==PAGE:4==
Abstract
In this thesis, we predict optical properties of solids from firstВӯprinciples calculations of the electric
susceptibility. The electric susceptibility contains important information about electronic excitaВӯ
tions in solids and thus is a key parameter when we want to describe how light interacts with and
propagates through matter. Accurate descriptions of lightВӯmatter interactions are crucial for the
design of many modern technologies within optics, photonics and material designs, that could for
instance be lasers, wave guides or solar cells. In addition to this, calculating the electric susceptiВӯ
bility accurately across a broad frequency range allows for correlation energy calculations via the
fluctuationВӯdissipation theorem [1] potentially improving density functional theory calculations
for solids, and predictions of all sorts of material properties. However, calculating the electric
susceptibility is no trivial task. Historically, it was introduced as a parameter that could only be
determined experimentally. Advances in quantum mechanics and manyВӯbody perturbation theory
have illuminated its nature, but exact calculations require that all exchange and correlation effects
be taken into account, a task that is impossible in practice. Approximate methods for determining
the electric susceptibility have been developed and have to some extent been successful in preВӯ
dicting optical spectra. The random phase approximation (RPA) neglects the exchangeВӯcorrelation
functional, but is computationally inexpensive and has proven to accurately describe plasmonic
excitations typically happening at higher frequencies in optical spectra. However, the neglect of
the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel means that excitonic excitations, mostly present at lower frequenВӯ
cies near the band gap, are not described in this theory. The BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE) on
the other hand gives an approximation to the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel and through this capВӯ
tures the excitons in the spectra. But the BSE is computationally costly, and it is only possible to
calculate the excitations at low frequencies with this method. The two methods each have differВӯ
ent strengths and weaknesses, but when excitons are present, none of them can give an accurate
description of the electric susceptibility over a broad range of frequencies. This project seeks to
bridge this gap by combining RPA and BSE calculations on the electric susceptibility in a hybrid
method. We propose four different hybrid methods denoted the Kernel Hybrid, the Simple Hybrid,
the Simple Hybrid ВӯH and Transition ВӯH. The hybrids are calculated for four different materials and
compared to experimental data both on high and low frequency spectra. For three of the materials,
we have also implemented the soВӯcalled fВӯsum method proposed by [2] to compare the hybrids to
this method. We show that all four hybrid methods do better than the RPA and BSE in predicting
optical spectra. We also compare the four methods to each other, and suggest some criteria for
when to apply each of them.

1
Introduction
The goal of this project is to predict optical properties of solids from firstВӯprinciples calculations of
the electric susceptibility. Electric susceptibility serves as a crucial parameter in physics, yielding
insights into the optical attributes of solids. One of the prominent properties that can be found from
the electric susceptibility is the refractive index, describing how light propagates through matter.
The refractive index gives us information about transmission, reflection, absorption and refraction
in the solid of interest. Thus, the refractive index needs to be determined when a research group
for instance would like to design optical fibers or solar cells where the path taken by the light is
important to obtain the desired effects in the technology in question. The electric susceptibility
also enables us to determine electron energy loss spectra (EELS), which gives information about
plasmons in the material. Plasmons, collective oscillations of the free electron gas density, have
significant implications for the optical properties of the material and the development of devices
using plasmonic phenomena. For instance, this understanding can enhance the design of plasmonic
lasers, which can be used for sensing and information technology [3].
From a more theoretical perspective, knowing the electric susceptibility over a wide range of freВӯ
quencies enables us to determine correlation energies. In density functional theory (DFT), which
in general is used for determining ground state energies and wave functions for complicated manyВӯ
particle systems, we need a ground state Hamiltonian describing the system. Some terms in this
Hamiltonian are known, e.g. the term corresponding to the kinetic energy, the potential energy due
to external fields, and Hartree interactions. But we need to add a soВӯcalled exchangeВӯcorrelation
functional. The exchangeВӯcorrelation functional is composed of the exchange energy, which is
exact in the HartreeВӯFock methods, and the correlation energy. Thus, particularly the correlaВӯ
tion energy, which accounts for electron correlation, the phenomenon where the movements and
properties of any one electron depends on the simultaneous properties and positions of all other
electrons, is something what we do not know. This term acts in a collective way, accounting for the
manyВӯbody interactions that remain after we have written down the terms that we know already.
But it is possible to show that it has a connection to the electric susceptibility via the fluctuationВӯ
dissipation theorem [1]. If we know the electric susceptibility up to very large frequencies, then,
in theory, we are able to determine the correlation energy. This would then enable us to perform
DFT calculations yielding more accurate wave functions and energies, in turn yielding more acВӯ
curate descriptions of solids. By performing computational characterizations of solids, ideally we
can help the experimentalists in their search for materials with specific properties needed in their
research.
In this project we perform calculations of the electric susceptibility over a wide range of frequenВӯ
cies. These calculations will be based on methods from linear response theory and manyВӯbody
perturbation theory, and will rely on ground state calculations from density functional theory.
Modelling these properties, one must account for the interactions between the particles in the solid.
Electrons and holes may combine to form excitons, thereby complicating the problem. Excitons
are well described by the BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE), but this equation is computationally exВӯ
pensive and not feasible for large systems where many electronic bands need to be taken into
account to achieve a broad frequency range and converged spectrum. A much simpler approach
is the Random Phase Approximation (RPA). This equation is numerically cheaper, but cannot
account for excitonic effects. However, it has proven successful in describing spectra at higher
frequencies. Plasmons can occur in the material due to the longВӯrange component of the Coulomb
interaction between the electrons in the material, and the RPA is capable of describing this effect.
In this project we aim at developing hybrid numerical approaches that combine the strengths of
Chapter 1. Introduction
1
==PAGE:8==
both these methods, capturing the important excitonic effects in a computationally feasible way in
order to accurately predict the electric susceptibility over a wide range of frequencies. The success
of the method is evaluated by comparison to experimental data on refractive indices, extinction coВӯ
efficients and EELS for four different materials, rutile TiO2, anatase TiO2, MoS22 and hexagonal
BN (hBN). We also predict the spectra for HfS2. Here we do not have experimental data, and thus
the predictions are made for experimental groups to confirm or reject in the future.
A large part of the project has been focused on understanding the extensive theory behind electric
susceptibility. Understanding the theory is crucial for interpreting results and handling problems
as they occur during the work. As a consequence, a large part of the thesis focuses on the theory
behind the calculations. The theory is covered in chapters 2Вӯ5 and is written at a level that should
be understandable for a reader unfamiliar with linear response theory and manyВӯbody perturbation
theory. A reader already acquainted with these topics may choose to skip parts of these chapters,
or read them more superficially.
In chapter 2 we give the classical definition of the electric susceptibility and explain its connection
to optical properties and spectra. We explain how to find absorption using the electric susceptibilВӯ
ity, and how absorption is connected to the soВӯcalled extinction coefficient. We also explain the
connection between the electric susceptibility and the dielectric function, and how the dielectric
function can be used to determine the refractive index. We introduce the physics behind two imporВӯ
tant types of electronic excitations that can sometimes be seen in optical spectra, namely excitons
and plasmons, and how to observe them experimentally.
In chapter 3 we dive further into the nature of the electric susceptibility through linear response
theory. This yields a quantum mechanical description of the electric susceptibility and through
Time Dependent DFT (TDDFT) we obtain a first mathematical expression for it. We also explain
the KramersВӯKronig relation which is obeyed by the electric susceptibility, and all other causal
response functions.
The TDDFT expression contains an exchangeВӯcorrelation functional which is unknown, and to get
further into the description, chapter 4 explains manyВӯbody perturbation theory. We derive the RPA
and explain its connection to the TDDFT expression. We then derive the BSE and explain how it
can be used to describe excitons in matter. We describe why the BSE is computationally heavy,
and how we can reduce the problem by using the TammВӯDancoff approximation.
In chapter 5 we propose four different hybrid approaches, combining the RPA and BSE calculaВӯ
tions in different ways. One of these hybrids makes use of the TDDFT expression for the electric
susceptibility.
In chapter 6 we show the results of our first investigations. We consider how using the TammВӯ
Dancoff approximation in the BSE changes the results for the electric susceptibility, and how to
improve the results under its limitations. The evaluations are done on bulk silicon. We show that
by careful consideration to the dynamics of the approximation, we are able to create very accurate
predictions for the optical spectra.
In chapter 7 we present the main results of this thesis. Here we show refractive indices and EELS
spectra found from electric susceptibilities calculated with the hybrids on four materials together
with experimental data. We compare the hybrid approaches, evaluate their strengths and weakВӯ
nesses, and discuss their use cases.
In chapter 8 we conclude on the work done in this project and discuss interesting future investigaВӯ
tions that can be made on the basis of the findings in this work.
Additional discussion of theory and results, as well as convergence tests for all calculations perВӯ
formed, may be found in appendix AВӯI.

Understanding Electric Susceptibility and

2.1
Absorption, refractive indices and extinction coefficients
This section takes a classical approach as a first step to explore the electric susceptibility. We will
investigate how the electric susceptibility can be employed to describe some wellВӯknown and meaВӯ
surable phenomena in nature by deriving the connections to some physical quantities known from
classical electromagnetism. This motivates our search for the electric susceptibility and enables us
to later compare our calculations to experimental data. The discussion in this section takes outset
in [4].
In general, we would like to describe how electric fields interact with and propagate through media.
For this, we need the Maxwell equations, which in matter read,
вҲҮВ· D = ПҒf
вҲҮВ· B = 0
вҲҮГ— E = вҲ’вҲӮB
вҲӮt
вҲҮГ— H = J + вҲӮD
вҲӮt ,
(2.1)
where E and B are the electric and magnetic fields respectively, ПҒf is the free charge density and
J is the current density. The HВӯfield is defined as H = B/Вө0 вҲ’M, where M is the magnetization
of the material and Вө0 = 4ПҖ В· 10вҲ’7 H/m is the vacuum permeability. In a linear medium the
magnetization is proportional to the HВӯfield,
M = ПҮMH,
(2.2)
where ПҮm is the magnetic susceptibility of the medium. Hence the magnetic field can be written
as,
B = Вө0ВөrH, where Вөr = 1 + ПҮm,
(2.3)
where Вөr is the relative permeability of the medium.
The DВӯfield, or the displacement field, is defined as
D = Оө0E + P,
(2.4)
where Оө0 = 8.85 В· 10вҲ’12 F/m is the vacuum permittivity and P is the polarization of the material.
The polarization accounts for the induced electric field of a dielectric medium due to an external
electric field and is defined as the electric dipole moment per unit volume. In a dielectric, the
electrons are bound to the atoms, but when subject to an electric field they will shift from their
average equilibrium positions, having the negatively charged electrons displaced opposite to the
direction of the electric field and the positively charged core displaced in the direction of the field.
Hence each atom constitutes a small electric dipole, and when summed up they create a macroВӯ
scopic polarization of the material. Thus, the external electric field makes the charges respond by
rearranging themselves creating an opposing field that partially cancels the external field. This
effect is also known as screening. In this way, eq. (2.4) implies that the EВӯfield, being the total
electrical field, is the external DВӯfield minus the induced field counteracting the external one.
In a linear, homogeneous and isotropic material the polarization is related to the electric field as,
P = Оө0ПҮE,
(2.5)
Chapter 2. Understanding Electric Susceptibility and Its Connection to Optical Spectra
3
==PAGE:10==
where ПҮ is the electric susceptibility of the material. And so ПҮ is a linear response function conВӯ
necting the polarization of the dielectric to the electric field. In this case, the displacement field
becomes
D = ОөE, where Оө = Оө0Оөr and Оөr = 1 + ПҮ.
(2.6)
We call Оө the permittivity and Оөr the dielectric function. The dielectric function is also called the
relative permittivity since it is defined as the ratio between the dielectric function and vacuum
permittivity. Оөr is related to the electric susceptibility as written in eq. (2.6), and thus we will
sometimes work with this quantity instead of directly with the electric susceptibility. Since Оөr
relates the total electric field to the external one, and the total electric field is the sum of the external
field and the polarization, it encodes the material response to an external field. Оөr, since measuring
the dielectric function relative to the vacuum permittivity, will be larger than 1, and thus eq. (2.6)
tells us that the external field will be larger than the total one, meaning that the material response
consists of a charge redistribution lowering the total field. That is, the charges redistribute in such
way that they screen the external field. Just like ПҮ, the dielectric function is then a linear response
function. We will return to this in the next section.
Note that we have encountered a limitation of this thesis. We aim at describing the linear response
of a material to an electrical field, but will not be able to account for nonlinear responses. We furВӯ
ther note that the electric and magnetic susceptibility, together with the dielectric function and the
relative permeability, are all material specific functions. They depend on the microscopic structure
of the material, and classically they would have to be determined experimentally.
Lets assume that we are in a medium with no free charges or currents, such that ПҒf = 0 and
J = 0. Substituting eq. (2.3) and (2.6) into the third and fourth equation of the Maxwell eqs. (2.1)
respectively, we then have that
вҲҮГ— E = вҲ’Вө0Вөr

2.1.1
Generalized theory
The theory presented above was simplified in order to present some points on how the electric
susceptibility contains information about material response to external fields. When we defined
the electric susceptibility and the dielectric function in eq. (2.5) and (2.6) we assumed that the
material in consideration was isotropic and homogeneous. We would like to generalize the theory.
In an anisotropic medium, ПҮ and Оө are tensors, and in an inhomogeneous medium we need to let ПҮ
and Оө be functions of the position.
Further, we will be using a slightly different formulation of the dielectric function than the one
given in eq. (2.6), which will also make more clear the physical interpretation of the displacement
field and the dielectric function. From the definition of the DВӯfield, eq. (2.4), we see that the total
electric field, the EВӯfield, is the DВӯfield minus the induced field corresponding to the polarization,
P. The total electric field must be the external field minus the induced one. Instead of speaking
directly of electric fields, the theory may also be expressed in terms of potentials. Also taking into
account that the material can be anisotropic and inhomogeneous, we may then instead write eq.
(2.6) as [5],
Оҙ(vtot(r, Пү)) =
Z
ОөвҲ’1
r (r, rвҖІ, Пү)vext(rвҖІ, Пү)drвҖІ.
(2.26)
Where Оҙ(vtot(r, Пү)) is a change in the total potential and vext is the external potential. ОөвҲ’1

the external potential and the change in the total potential, and thus it encodes the material response
in the form of a screening process as also described in the previous section.
2.2
Electronic excitations in optical spectra
As was found out in the previous section, the electric susceptibility enables us to describe the
absorption of a dielectric. In this section we describe some types of electronic excitations, namely
excitons and plasmons, that can sometimes be observed in optical spectra. We will later need this
theory to describe strengths and weaknesses of models for finding the electric susceptibility and to
understand and interpret obtained results.
2.2.1
Excitons
When an incoming particle, that could be a photon, excites an electron from the valence band into
the conduction band creating a hole in the valence band, the electron and hole are bound together
by the screened attractive Coulomb interaction W between them. The electronВӯhole pair is what
we call an exciton [4]. Screening refers to the fact, that other charged particles surrounding the
electron/hole cancels some of the Coulomb potential binding the exciton together. When light
excites electrons from the valence bands into the conduction bands, the frequency of the light will
match that energy difference between the two bands. And so, normally, the incoming photon will
have an energy of at least the band gap in order to excite the system, but the attractive potential
between the pair lowers the energy, and thus we are able to observe excitonic peaks at energies
less than that of the band gap. Note however that excitons also may lie above the band gap, thus
6
2.2. Electronic excitations in optical spectra
==PAGE:13==
affecting the spectrum at larger energies too. But at large enough energies, the binding energy
becomes too weak, and the effect of excitons becomes negligible. There is not a clear point at
which this happens. In a first description, the exciton can be modelled as a hydrogenВӯlike system.
The energy of the excitons inside the band gap, En, is then given as [4],
En = Eg вҲ’Вө
m0
1
Оө2r
RH
n2 ,
(2.27)
where Eg is the energy of the band gap, and the last term is the excitation energy of the excitons,
similar to that of the hydrogen atom. However, since we describe the excitons in matter and not in
free space, the vacuum permittivity is replaced by the dielectric function of the medium in which
the excitons are, and the mass of a free electron, m0, is replaced by the effective mass, Вө, of the
exciton. RH is the Rydberg constant and n is an integer, revealing that the excitons create a series of
separate peaks in the spectrum. Excitonic peaks below the band gap can for example be observed
experimentally in bulk MoS2 as seen in figure 2.1 found by [6] using soВӯcalled spectroscopic
ellipsometry.
Figure 2.1: Experimentally found imaginary part of the dielectric function for Bulk MoS2. The
two rather sharp peaks at low frequencies are excitons. Data obtained from [6].
The exciton can move through the crystal transporting energy. The strength and stability of the
exciton is determined by multiple conditions. The pair is more strongly bound if they have the
same group velocityНҫ otherwise they will drift apart while moving through the crystal. The group
velocity is given as
vG = 1
Д§
вҲӮE
вҲӮk ,
(2.28)
which implies that we will see more strongly bound excitons when the valence and conduction
bands are parallel. Secondly, the screened attractive potential between the electron and hole needs
to be strong enough to bind the pair and to protect it against collisions with phonons. The maximal
energy of a phonon is on the order kBT, and so the binding energy should exceed this. Therefore,
we are more likely to see excitonic peaks in the spectrum at low temperatures. This effect is for
example seen in GaAs in figure 2.2 obtained from [7]. It is important to note, that also at higher
temperatures do the excitons affect the spectrum, but they are less pronounced as they are more
loosely bound. Neglecting excitonic effects, it can be derived classically that the absorption should
be proportional to the square root of the energy [4], but due to excitons at room temperature, the
initial rise in absorption will be somewhat more abrupt than this.
2.2. Electronic excitations in optical spectra
7
==PAGE:14==
Figure 2.2: Experimentally found absorption spectrum for GaAs. The round dots are from an exВӯ
periment at 294 K, the squares at 186 K, the triangles at 90 K and the dots at 21 K. We clearly see
how the excitonic peak becomes more pronounced at lower temperatures. At about room temperВӯ
ature, we do however also see a more sharp rise in the absorption spectrum than the theoretically
predicted square root behaviour. Figure obtained from [7].
The binding energy depends on how strongly the electron and hole interact through the screened
Coulomb interaction. If the screening in the material is small, the screened coulomb interaction
between them is larger, and they are more likely to form a stable exciton. If, on the other hand,
the screening is very large, they are only weakly bound Вӯ if at all Вӯ and will not see them in the
spectrum. That is the case for metals, and thus we do not observe excitons in metals. The Coulomb
attraction between the electron and hole means that they are drawn closer together which increases
the overlap between their wave functions. FermiвҖҷs Golden rule then implies, that this causes an
increase in transitions rate, yielding more intense absorption [4]. And so, excitonic absorption
peaks can be quite large compared to the continuous part of the spectrum.
In general, we distinguish between two types of excitons, the Frenkel and the MottВӯWannier. The
Frenkel excitons are tightly bound excitons, and often the electron and hole will be on the same
atom. These are very localized excitons and if they are to move through the material it is done
by hopping between neighboring atoms. They are typically observed in rare gas crystals, alkali
halides and molecular crystals [4]. The MottВӯWannier excitons on the other are more weakly bound
excitons and can consist of an electron in the conduction band and a hole in the valence band of
semiconductors [4]. These are the excitons that we consider in this project.
A way of modelling excitons in the spectrum is through the BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE). We
will go much more into depth with the BSE and its derivation in section 4.4. But to get the a first
glimpse of the physics, we shortly consider a 1D chain of atoms forming a valence and a conduction
band, following the note of [8]. The system can be modelled with a Hamiltonian, H, consisting
of a nonВӯinteracting and interacting part, H0 and HвҖІ, both consisting of the operators, ЛҶbвҖ /ЛҶb which,
when having the subscript c, creates/annihilates an electron in the conduction bands, and when
having the subscript v creates/annihilates a hole in the valence band. The nonВӯinteracting part is a
tight binding Hamiltonian with nearest neighbor hopping,
H0 = E0 вҲ’

no excitonic peaks occur. But when it is stronger, U = 10, we see some excitation peaks below the
continuum of excitations, which are excitations from the valence to the conduction bandsНҫ these
discrete peaks are excitons.
2.2.2
Plasmons
At larger excitation energies, under the right conditions, it is possible to observe soВӯcalled plasВӯ
monic peaks. In principle, we also have excitons at these larger energies, but they are less visible
due to screening, and instead the dominant feature is the plasmons. In a plasma, we have a colВӯ
lection of charges in equilibrium, but if a region with excess charge is created, it will be pushed
away from its original position by the surrounding charges. The velocity achieved by this process
will make the charges overshoot their original position and create an oscillating movement. This is
what we call plasma oscillations: these are collections of longitudinal excitations of the electrons
in the material, and a quantum of this is what we call a plasmon [4].
In a material with no average charge density, the first of the Maxwell eqs., (2.1), reads
вҲҮВ· (Оө0ОөrE) = 0,
(2.33)
2.2. Electronic excitations in optical spectra
9
==PAGE:16==
which is satisfied for transversely polarized waves. But if Оөr = 0 the material can carry longitudiВӯ
nally polarized waves, and this is therefore the condition under which we can observe plasmons.
In a simple Lorentz oscillator model, it is possible to show that an approximation to the plasma
frequency is [4],
Пүp =
 nee2
Оө0m0

,
(2.34)
where m0 is the effective mass and ne is the electron density. Considering plasmons we often
think about metals since these can be modeled as free electron gases with the potential for makВӯ
ing this collective longitudinal oscillation. But since we would like to study materials with more
complex spectra in this project, by which we mean that at lower energies we also observe excitons,
we would really like to consider plasmons in semiconductors. Overall, the dynamics behind the
semiconductor plasmons are the same as in metals. But in metals, all electrons and holes are in
the conduction band, and so, when the system gets excited at the plasma frequency, the excitation
is an intraband transition. In semiconductors on the other hand, the electrons in the valence band
have to be excited across the band gap, and it is instead an interband excitation that constitutes the
plasmon [9]. The mathematical description of the plasmons is then a bit more complex since we
cannot just use the free electron gas model, and also because plasmons at lower frequencies can
be influenced by excitons. In [9] they propose a better model for plasmons in undoped semiconВӯ
ductors. It is also possible that free charges e.g. from doping or impurities, can create metalВӯlike
plasmons in the semiconductor, or that we are able to excite surface plasmons [4]. A more detailed
description is beyond the scope of this thesis, but more information can be found in [9] and [4].
It is the longВӯrange part of the Coulomb interaction which is responsible for the creation of plasВӯ
mons [10]. This statement seems reasonable since the plasmonic oscillation is a collective reВӯ
sponse, thus relying on the charges interacting across longer distances. The BSE, that was shortly
mentioned in the section above, does in principle contain this longВӯrange component of the Coulomb
interaction and thus, in theory, it is able to capture plasmonic effects. However, there are two
problems in regard to describing plasmons with the BSE. One is that since the method is comВӯ
putationally expensive, we are only able to include a few bands in the calculations. The plasВӯ
mons are typically present at larger energies, and thus, to capture the plasmonic response, we need
many electronic bands in the calculation, making the BSE unsuited for this type of investigation.
Figure 2.4: Plot (a) is an experimenВӯ
tally found EELS spectrum for bulk silВӯ
icon. We see a clear plasmonic peak
at around 18 eV. Figure obtained from
[11]
Further, as will become clearer later on, the BSE makes
use of the TammВӯDancoff approximation, which works
better if the longВӯrange component of the Coulomb interВӯ
action is taken out of the calculations [10]. However, the
plasmons are also captured by another manyВӯbody perВӯ
turbation method, namely the Random Phase ApproxiВӯ
mation (RPA), which is a mean field method much less
computationally costly. The RPA too contains the longВӯ
range Coulomb interaction and is then capable of capturВӯ
ing the plasmonic effects.
The plasmons, and if we are at low energies also the exciВӯ
tons, can be observed through electron energy loss specВӯ
troscopy (EELS). See for example figure 2.4 obtained
from [11] displaying some experimental EELS data for
silicon. In EELS, electrons at different energies are emitВӯ
ted onto the sample and by measuring the reduced enerВӯ
gies and momenta of the electrons after they have interВӯ
acted with the material, one obtains a spectrum that conВӯ
tains peaks at the plasma frequency Пүp [2]. In qВӯresolved
10
2.2. Electronic excitations in optical spectra
==PAGE:17==
EELS, the momentum transfer can be found by measuring the deflection angle after the electrons
have interacted with the sample. The electrons transfer momenta corresponding to different wave
vectors q. The transferred energy for some specific q is then the initial energy of the electron minus
the final energy. EELS that are not qВӯresolved contain the sum of all momentum transfers. In this
project, we have only considered photons, which implies zero momentum transfer q = 0. Thus,
ideally, we would like to obtain data from qВӯresolved EELS such that we can consider q = 0 specВӯ
tra. However, it has not always been possible to find qВӯresolved EELS, and nonВӯqВӯresolved EELS
is then considered to be better than no benchmarking at all. Especially since the q = 0 spectrum
is dominant in the spectrum compared to other qВӯvectors.
EELS is related to the dielectric function [2],
EELS = вҲ’Im

r (q, Пү)

.
(2.35)
Since the longitudinal oscillations are only possible at Оөr = 0, incoming light with frequencies
corresponding to the plasma frequencies, Пү = Пүp, will create strong peaks in the EELS spectrum.
The dielectric function is a matrix and in reciprocal space, the matrix entries correspond to differВӯ
ent reciprocal lattice vectors, G, GвҖІ. Since we are searching for longВӯrange responses in order to
capture plasmonic effects, we are only interested in the macroscopic component corresponding to
G = GвҖІ = 0 in eq. (2.35).
2.3
Summary
In this section we saw that a medium will respond to an applied external field by redistributing
the charges and thereby creating a net polarization. In the case where we have a linear material,
the polarization is related to the total electric field through the electric susceptibility ПҮ. Thus, the
electric susceptibility describes a linear response of the system corresponding to redistributions of
charge densities as a result of the electric field present in the media creating an overall polarization.
Likewise, the dielectric function ОөвҲ’1
r
is a linear response function relating the total potential to the
externally applied one encoding how charges will redistribute in order to screen the externally
applied field. We have seen that these two quantities are related to each other, and that they enable
us to find important optical quantities like the absorption, extinction coefficient, refractive index
and EELS spectra. Finally we described that optical spectra can be influence by bound electronВӯ
hole pairs called excitons creating excitation states below the band gap of the material. We also
described plasmons, being collective, longitudinal charge oscillations.
Both the electric susceptibility and the dielectric function describe the response of charges inside
the material, i.e. they describe dynamical processes of particles governed by the laws of quantum
mechanics. In electromagnetism the particles are treated as classical objects with definite posiВӯ
tion and momenta and they only interact through electromagnetic fields. However, to accurately
account for the material response, we need to describe the system using time dependent quantum
mechanics. Thus we need to investigate time dependent quantum mechanics within the framework
of linear response theory. This is what we will do in the next chapter.
2.3. Summary
11
==PAGE:18==
12
2.3. Summary
==PAGE:19==
3
Linear Response Theory and TDDFT:
Defining Electric Susceptibility
In this section we dive deeper into the physics behind the electric susceptibility and the dielectric
function. In the previous section we saw how they are both defined as linear response functions, and
we will now look into the consequences of this and how it enables us to create equations for them,
thus working beyond classical electromagnetism where they could only be found experimentally.
In this section we therefore develop the tools for describing time dependence in quantum mechanВӯ
ics. We give the Kubo formula, and we show how this leads to an expression for the electric
susceptibility. We consider the expression in the nonВӯinteracting case. And we derive a connecВӯ
tion to the dielectric function. Often the systems in consideration will have periodic structures, so
we rewrite the expressions for the electric susceptibility and the dielectric function for this case.
We finally show that the electric susceptibility obeys the KramersВӯKronig relation and discuss the
consequences of that.
3.1
Time dependence in quantum mechanics
In time dependent quantum mechanics we would like to describe how states and observables repreВӯ
sented by hermitian operators develop as time progresses. To describe the time dependence of the
system, we generally work within three pictures, the Heisenberg picture, the SchrГ¶dinger picture
and the Interaction picture. In the following, we use the definitions from [12].
In the SchrГ¶dinger picture we assume that the Hamiltonian does not depend on time. The time
dependence of the states can be found as,
|ПҲ(t)вҹ©= eвҲ’i ЛҶHt|ПҲ0вҹ©,
(3.1)
where we are working in natural units, i.e. Д§ = 1. The operators may or may not depend on time,
but their time dependence is not described within this picture. Thus, it is mostly useful to apply
this picture if the operators are time independent.
In the Heisenberg picture, the Hamiltonian is still assumed to not depend on time. The states are
time independent, and the time dependence of the operators can be found as
ЛҶA(t) = ei ЛҶHt ЛҶAeвҲ’i ЛҶHt.
(3.2)
Both the Heisenberg and SchrГ¶dinger picture however assumed that the Hamiltonian did not deВӯ
pend on time. In general, the quantum mechanical objects in our material will be interacting with
each other and the surroundings and therefore the Hamiltonian will be evolving in time. To deal
with this, the interaction picture separates the Hamiltonian, H into a nonВӯinteracting, H0, and inВӯ
teracting part, V (t), which can be time dependent,
ЛҶH = ЛҶH0 + ЛҶV (t).
(3.3)
In the interaction picture, the time dependence of the operators can simply be described by the
nonВӯinteracting part of the Hamiltonian,
ЛҶAI(t) = ei ЛҶH0tAIeвҲ’i ЛҶH0t,
(3.4)

action picture offers a simple time dependence of the operators at the expense of a complicated
time dependence of the states. Thus, the time dependence of the states in this picture must be
approximated. For this, we use linear response theory.

The expression describes how the density in the point r changes due to a perturbation in the density
in some other point rвҖІ [13]. Therefore, we rename the electric susceptibility the densityВӯdensity
response function, or, in short, the density response function. Eq. (3.15) is then the density response
function in the Lehmann representation, referring to the fact that we have used the eigenstates |sвҹ©
of the unperturbed Hamiltonian as basis set. The poles in the expression, Пү = Вұ(Es вҲ’E0) are
the excitation energies of the system. If the frequency is the plasma frequency, Пү = Пүp, then |sвҹ©is
the plasma wave function. Although we now know this formal expression for the density response
function in eq. (3.15), we are in general not able to determine the manyВӯbody eigenstates |sвҹ©for
the unperturbed Hamiltonian ЛҶH0. If there are no interactions between the electrons, the states |sвҹ©
are however single particle eigenstates and we may write them in terms of Slater determinants.
Therefore we will now determine an expression for the nonВӯinteracting density response function,
ПҮ0, from eq. (3.15), containing only elements that we are actually able to calculate.
3.2.1
NonВӯinteraction density response function
Still with the outset in [13], we determine the nonВӯinteracting density response function in this
section. Initially, we use that the number density operator is defined as
ЛҶn(r) = ЛҶОЁвҖ (r)ЛҶОЁ(r),
(3.16)
where ЛҶОЁвҖ  and ЛҶОЁ are operators creating and annihilating position eigenvectors respectively,
ЛҶОЁвҖ (r)|0вҹ©= |rвҹ©.

In the nonВӯinteracting case we are able to actually calculate the density response function. The
states entering the expression are just single particle eigenstates for the singleВӯparticle Hamiltonian
which we may write up using Slater determinants, and knowing the singleВӯparticle Hamiltonian and
the states, we can find the eigenenergies by solving the singleВӯparticle SchrГ¶dinger equation. In
this project, we will find the states and energies using Density functional theory (DFT) to obtain
the nonВӯinteracting KohnВӯSham singleВӯparticle orbitals which are then used in the expressions for
the density response functions. In DFT, the ground state energies and wave functions are found
through a selfВӯconsistency problem which can be solved iteratively. The method relies on a DFT
Hamiltonian, which contains an exchangeВӯcorrelation potential. There have been developed some
different exchangeВӯcorrelation functions to approximate it. In this project we have been using
the PBE, which is explained in [14]. An explanation of the theory behind DFT can be found in
appendix B.

ПөвҲ’1

The different components GGвҖІ in eq. (3.40) give us information on the variations of the dielectric
function on different length scales. But the external potential will vary on a length scale much
larger than that of the unit cell. The external potential will, however, induce fluctuations on all
length scales of the system. These fluctuations induce local potentials that also have microscopic
components, and even though this will not be resolved experimentally, it will affect the long waveВӯ
length component of the total potential and thus indirectly affect the experiment [2]. In the end,
what will be measured is the component corresponding to G = GвҖІ = 0 and thus we define the
macroscopic dielectric function [5],
ПөM(Пү) = lim
qвҶ’0
1
ПөвҲ’1
00 (q, Пү).
(3.41)
We call other components with G Мё= 0 and GвҖІ Мё= 0, local field effects. These arise from microscopic
variations in the material [2]. To neglect local field effects corresponds to neglecting offВӯdiagonal
elements of ПөGGвҖІ. In this case, the matrix inversion performed when finding the macroscopic
dielectric function in eq. (3.41) will just correspond to finding the inverse value of the G = GвҖІ = 0Вӯ
component, and we will have that ПөM(Пү) = limqвҶ’0 Пө00(q, Пү). To account for local field effects
affecting the G = GвҖІ = 0Вӯcomponent we need to perform the matrix inversion before taking the
G = GвҖІ = 0Вӯcomponent.
Consider once again eq. (3.40). In this project, we consider optical transitions. Compared to
electrons, photons have very small momenta relative to their energy. And therefore, to a good
approximation, we can neglect the momentum transfer in optical transitions, which implies that
q = 0. Thus, if G = GвҖІ = 0, the Coulomb interaction being proportional to 1/|G + q|2 Вӯ see
appendix C Вӯ will diverge. However, using perturbation theory, it can shown that in fact the matrix
elements in (3.38) will be proportional to q2 thus cancelling the divergence.

3.2. Linear response theory and the Kubo formula
==PAGE:27==
Note that all one has to do to obtain the polarizability, ВҜПҮ, instead of the density response function,
ПҮ, in eq. (3.46) is then to put to longВӯrange component of the Coulomb interaction to zero! At
first sight, working with the polarizability instead of the density response function may seem a
bit redundant, but in chapters 6, 7 we will most often work with the polarizability, rather than
the density response function directly. This is partly due to the fact that the polarizability is linked
more directly to the absorption and thus it allows us to omit some conversion steps, but also, as will
be shown in chapter 6, because the TammВӯDancoff approximations works better when excluding
the longВӯrange component of the Coulomb interaction. Another important point is that from eq.
(3.42), it is apparent that we can determine the EELS spectra from the polarizability, even though it
does not contain the longВӯrange component of the Coulomb interaction which is otherwise needed
in order for us to observe plasmons in the spectrum. We will just need to invert ОөM as stated in the
formula (2.35). So when determining EELS spectra later in this thesis, chapter 7, this is exactly
what we do.
3.3
The KramersВӯKronig relation
The dielectric function and the density response function are, as the name suggests, response funcВӯ
tions and the real and imaginary part are bound by causality. This is what the KramersВӯKronig
relation encodes. To derive this relation, we consider the polarization as stated initially in eq. (2.5)
but as a function of time [15],

(3.56)
This is the KramersВӯKronig relation yielding the connection between the real and imaginary part
of the density response function. The transformation that relates the real and imaginary parts is
denoted a Hilbert transformation. The fact that the outset of the derivation consisted of imposing
causality by multiplying the density response function by the Heaviside function, reveals that the
real and imaginary part are bound to each other by causality. Thus, we may check if a given density
response function is causal without having to transform back to the time domain. An important
point is that the real part of a response function depends on the entire spectrum of the imaginary
part. Adding a peak in the imaginary spectrum affects the real part on all frequencies. This effect
is shown in figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: 6 Lorenzian peaks added in the order indicated on the plot of the imaginary part to the
left. Every time a new peak is added, a superposition between this peak and the peaks already in
the plot is created. In the end, when all peaks are added, the superposition is constituted by the
black curve. The real part on the plot to the right shows the Hilbert transform of the imaginary
part. I.e. initially, when we only have the red peak, the real part is the red curve on the right plot.
When we add the green peak, the superposition of the red and green peak has the real part plotted
with green, and so on. Thus, finally, when the yellow curve is added, we obtain the superposition
marked with the black curve on the left plot, and therefore, the real part on the right plot is marked
with both yellow and black.
3.4
TDDFT
Up until now we have derived an exact expression for the density response function, eq. (3.15),
and its connection to the dielectric function, eq. (3.29) in real space and (3.40) in frequency space.
However, we are unable to solve the SchrГ¶dinger equation for interacting systems in order to obВӯ
tain the energies and wave functions entering these expressions. Therefore we decided to derive
expressions for the nonВӯinteraction density response function in real space, eq. (3.24), and in freВӯ
quency space, eq. (3.38). Here, the wave functions can be written as Slater determinants, and we
22
3.4. TDDFT
==PAGE:29==
can find the eigenenergies by solving the SchrГ¶dinger equation with these states and the singleВӯ
particle nonВӯinteracting Hamiltonian. In this project, actually determining eigenstates and energies
for the density response functions is done using DFT which is explained in appendix B. Employing
timeВӯdependent DFT (TDDFT), we will now find a connection between the nonВӯinteracting and
interacting density response function derived in the previous section. We will follow the derivation
in [5]. Thus, even though the nonВӯinteracting density response function at first sight seemed of litВӯ
tle use, we may find a formal connection to the full interacting one thereby justifying our interest
in it. As we will see, this otherwise exact expression that we will be arriving at, contains elements
that we are unable to actually calculate. Therefore we will in chapter 4 proceed to manyВӯbody
perturbation theory to explore the content of the terms and from this derive approximations.
This section follows the note of [5]. To determine a connection between the interacting and nonВӯ
interacting density response function the idea in TDDFT is to create an effective potential in the
form [5],

This expression is in principle exact, but we do not know the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel, eq.
(3.66). Exchange refers to the antiВӯsymmetry of the wavefunction which is due to Pauli exclusion
principle, and correlation is sort of a collective term accounting for the remaining manyВӯbody
interactions. As this suggests, determining the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel is no trivial task. In
order to gain more insight into dynamics of this kernel, we will introduce manyВӯbody perturbation
theory.
3.5
Summary
In this section we focused on linear response theory. We assumed that the system in consideration
was subject to a small perturbative potential, and saw how the expectation value of an operator
changed under this perturbation. From this we could then determine how the expectation value
of the density operator changed when subject to a potential, and this yielded the density response
function, eq. (3.15). We saw how response functions are restricted to obey the KramersВӯKronig
relation. However, in a general quantum mechanical system with interacting electrons we are not
able to actually calculate the density response function since it contains the manyВӯbody eigenstates
|sвҹ©of the unperturbed Hamiltonian ЛҶH0. We derived an expression for the nonВӯinteracting density
response function containing only elements that we can actually determine, eq. (3.24). We saw
how to reformulate the response function in a periodic system, eq. (3.38), and we discussed the
effect of local fields. In order to find the eigenvalues and Вӯstates for the nonВӯinteracting density
response function, we use DFT to obtain the nonВӯinteracting KohnВӯSham singleВӯparticle orbitals,
and DFT is explained in appendix B. We used TDDFT to derive a connection between the nonВӯ
interacting and interacting density response function, eq. (3.68). This expression contains the
Hartree potential and the nonВӯinteracting density response function, which we are indeed able to
determine, but it also contains the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel, which is not a known quantity. In
the next section we will employ manyВӯbody perturbation theory in order to eventually gain insight
into this exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel.
24
3.5. Summary
==PAGE:31==
4
ManyВӯBody Perturbation Theory: General
Formalism and Derivations of the RPA and
BSE
In this section we will investigate an alternative derivation of the density response function using
manyВӯbody perturbation theory. This formulation yields further insight into the nature and dynamВӯ
ics of the density response function and enable us to find approximate methods for modelling the
interactions in the systems contained in the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel.
Initially, we will introduce manyВӯbody perturbation theory including the singleВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs
functions, imaginary time and Matsubara GreenвҖҷs functions and Wicks theorem. From this we
then show how to obtain an expression for the density response function in the random phase
approximation (RPA). We discuss the limitations of this approximation and then derive a more
accurate expression, the BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE), for the density response function. We
finally discuss the challenges connected to this equation.
4.1
Single particle GreenвҖҷs functions
In manyВӯbody problems we would ideally like to solve the SchrГ¶dinger equation to obtain eigenВӯ
values and Вӯstates to determine the density response function eq. (3.15). Having interactions, this
is not possible, but we may instead describe the system using single particle GreenвҖҷs functions.
Following derivations and definitions from [12], we here describe the evolution and propagation
of a single particle governed by a manyВӯbody Hamiltonian.
In general we define four different GreenвҖҷs functions, the advanced, retarded, lesser and greater

the distance between them increases. The Coulomb force is, as discussed in chapter 2, responsible
for the formation of bound states and the emergence of collective excitations such as plasmons.

When three lines meet at an internal vertex, momentum conservation leaves one of the momenta

is reduced to determining the selfВӯenergy containing the irreducible Feynman diagrams without
external fermion lines. However, this is still an infinite series, and therefore we will have to find
approximations to it. For this, we apply the random phase approximation.
4.3
Random phase approximation
In order to approximate the selfВӯenergy, eq. (4.28), the idea in RPA is to only include the most
divergent diagrams assuming that we are in the limit of high particle densities. We continue to
follow [12].
30
4.3. Random phase approximation
==PAGE:37==
It can be shown, that the most divergent diagrams are those to lowest order in the expansion.

dimensionless if we were to measure them in powers of the Fermi momentum kF . For a selfВӯenergy
to an arbitrary order n, there are n internal momenta. Each momentum corresponds to an integral
over k and a sum over ikn originating from the Fourier transform. We also have a 1/ОІ = kbT in

1 вҲ’vC(q)ПҮ0(q, Пү)
вҮ’ОөRPA = 1 вҲ’vC(q)ПҮ0(q, Пү).
(4.53)
Then, using eq. (3.25) in reciprocal space for a translation invariant system, we have
vtot(q, Пү)RPA =
1
ОөRPA vext =
vext
1 вҲ’vC(q)ПҮ0(q, Пү).
(4.54)
Notice the resemblance between eq. (4.51) and (4.54). The density response function describes
the change in the density at some point r, i.e. the polarization of the system due to a change in the
charge density in some other point rвҖІ, while the dielectric function describes the screening response
of the system due to an external field. The similarity between the two equations reveals that the
response of the system will be the same, no matter if the perturbation comes from the charges not
being in equilibrium or from an external field. In both cases, the charges will redistribute to screen
the field.
To sum up, in RPA, the selfВӯenergy is approximated by an infinite perturbative expansion retainВӯ
ing only the most divergent Feynman diagrams. This series then only consists of pairВӯbubbles, a
pairВӯbubble corresponding to an electronВӯhole pair being created and annihilated again in a virtual
process. This process can be considered to be a polarization of the system as a response to the
charged particle interacting through the Coulomb interaction or interacting with an external field.
Through the approximation of the selfВӯenergy, the RPA allows us to obtain an expression for the
interacting density response function using only pair bubbles, consisting of the nonВӯinteracting
density response function and the Coulomb interaction, eq. (4.51). Thus, even though the nonВӯ
interacting density response function maybe at first seemed of little use, we now see that it can be
used in RPA to model interactions.
The response in the form of a system polarization leads to screened Coulomb interactions between
charged particles in the system. This is indeed a physically sound response: an electron will attract
holes which then partially cancel the field of the electron, and thus the electron will only interact
with other charged particles in the system though a weakened Coulomb interaction.
However, if we would like to describe the interaction between two specific charged particles, the
RPA is not of any help. The RPA describes how a single charged particle interacts with a mean
electric field generated by charged particles in its surroundings through the Hartree potential. But
it is unable to describe the interaction between two charged particles, and thus cannot predict the
presence of excitons in the absorption spectrum. Excitonic behaviour is generated by the soВӯcalled
34
4.3. Random phase approximation
==PAGE:41==
vertex corrections which are neglected in RPA. This is something that we will return to in the next
section.
However, the RPA does contain the longВӯrange variation of the Hartree potential, G = GвҖІ =
0, and this interaction is responsible for collective charge oscillations giving rise to plasmonic
effects. Thus, RPA does well describe the plasmonic effects in the material happening typically
at frequencies well above the band gap. At these larger frequencies we may also have some weak
excitonic effects, but the spectrum will often dominated by the plasmons, and the RPA will typically
yield satisfying predictions for the spectra, as we will also see in chapter 7.
Importantly, we have gained insight into the physics of the density response function originally
derived in section 3.4 about TDDFT, eq. (3.68). Here, the full density response function contains
three terms: the first term is the nonВӯinteracting density response function, the second term conВӯ
tains the Hartree interaction and the third contains the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel. The first two
terms are exactly the expression derived in RPA, eq. (4.51). Thus, these terms correspond to the
electrons of the system interacting with each other and external fields through the Coulomb interВӯ
action thereby creating a polarization, which is consistent with the assumptions made in order to
derive eq. (3.68), i.e. the first term containing the bare nonВӯinteracting density response function
came from the term in the effective field containing the external field, and the term containing the
Coulomb interaction came from the Hartree potential which is a potential mimicking the Coulomb
interaction. The term containing the exchangeВӯinteraction kernel on the hand is neglected in RPAНҫ
these are all the Feynman diagrams containing other interactions than those described by pairВӯ
bubbles. Thus, an important weakness of the RPA is the lack of exchange and correlation effects.
To obtain some of the dynamics from these diagrams, we need to find a way of including some of
them. This is exactly what we will do in the BetheВӯSalpeter equation.
4.4
BetheВӯSalpeter equation
To go beyond RPA we need to add the soВӯcalled vertex corrections which is done by solving the
fourВӯpoint BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE). In this section, we derive the BSE. The BSE will depend
on an exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel which we will need to approximate with the soВӯcalled GWВӯ
approximation justified by the Hedin equations. We consider an important reformulation of the
BSE, and then consider the scaling problems in this equation and how the problem is simplified
by imposing the TammВӯDancoff approximation.
In RPA, we considered the polarization created by electrons and holes interacting only through
the Hartree potential, and so we did not include exchangeВӯcorrelation effects. Since there were
no direct interactions, using the singleВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs functions was sufficient for describing the
singleВӯparticle excitations that can occur. But now we would like to also describe more complex exВӯ
citations with excitons, which involve the correlated motion of an electronВӯhole pair. TwoВӯparticle
GreenвҖҷs functions are a natural generalization of the singleВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs functions to describe
twoВӯparticle excitations. Thus, we need twoВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs functions to describe the system. In
the following, we will in general take outset in [10].
When the RPA fails in describing excitons, it is because it is a mean field method approximating the
direct Coulomb interaction with the Hartree potential, as we saw both in section 4.3 about the RPA,
and also in section 3.4 about TDDFT. Excitons are bound together by the Coulomb interaction, and
therefore we need the explicit Coulomb potential, eq. (4.12), in our Hamiltonian in order to catch
this effect. LetвҖҷs consider what happens if we just naively add the Coulomb interaction in the
Hamiltonian. We then have a general Hamiltonian on the form [10],

If we were to continue the EOM technique, the next equation would contain a threeВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs

in order to model direct Coulomb interaction between two particles Вӯ which of course makes sense
since the Coulomb interaction is a twoВӯbody interaction Вӯ but the derivation reveals that we are
unable to determine it with the EOM technique. A description of the density response function
containing the information from the twoВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs function is necessary if we want to have
excitons in the calculated spectra.
We define the twoВӯparticle correlation function 4ПҮ(1, 1вҖІ, 2, 2вҖІ) as the twoВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs function
minus the uncorrelated contribution from the singleВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs function describing each of
the particle propagation separately and independently,
4ПҮ(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) = вҲ’G2(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) + G(1, 1вҖІ)G(2, 2вҖІ).

4ПҮ(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) = ОҙG(1, 1вҖІ)

where we used the expression for the density in eq. (4.67), and we also used that the nonВӯlocal

Imagine we would like to draw this with a Feynman diagram. If a GreenвҖҷs function can be repВӯ

making the diagram for the nonВӯcorrelated twoВӯparticle function resemble a pair bubble. This turns
our thoughts to the nonВӯinteracting density response function. However, the two GreenвҖҷs functions
entering eq. (4.78) are not necessarily the nonВӯinteracting GreenвҖҷs functions as in the expression
for ПҮ0. If we approximate the GreenвҖҷs function in eq. (4.78) with the nonВӯinteracting GreenвҖҷs
functions then we obtain the nonВӯinteracting density response function. In that case eq. (4.78)
becomes,
4ПҮnc(1, 2, 1+, 2+) = G(1, 2+)G(2, 1+) вүҲG0(1, 2+)G0(2, 1+) = ПҮ0(1, 2).
(4.79)
Thus, if we put 1вҖІ = 1+ and 2вҖІ = 2+, neglect the vertex correction by taking ОҙОЈxc/ОҙG = 0 and
make the approximation of the singleВӯparticle GreenвҖҷs function as just described, we recover the
RPA Dyson equation for ПҮ, eq. (4.51), from the BSE (4.75).
While this is all very nice, we have not at this point gotten much further from the TDDFT descripВӯ
tion. We still have an exchangeВӯcorrelation contribution in the form of the vertex correction which
we do not know. To go further, we need the Hedin equations and the GWВӯapproximation.
Hedin equations
The Hedin equations are integral equations that can be used to calculate the GreenвҖҷs function, selfВӯ
energy, polarizability and vertex correction. A derivation of the Hedin equations can be found in
appendix F.
The Hedin equations consist of a Dyson equation for the irreducible vertex ЛңО“, with the first term
being a local contribution corresponding to the HartreeВӯFock selfВӯenergy and the last term being the
vertex correction. The second equation is an expression for the exchangeВӯcorrelation selfВӯenergy,
the third is the irreducible density response function, or polarizability, and the fourth is an equation
for the screened interaction. The fifth Hedin equation is simply the Dyson equation for the GreenвҖҷs
function, which we have seen before. The five Hedin equations are [10]:
ЛңО“(1, 2, 3) = Оҙ(1, 3)Оҙ(2, 3) + ОҙОЈxc(1, 2)
ОҙG(ВҜ5вҖІ, ВҜ5) G(ВҜ5вҖІ, ВҜ6)G(ВҜ6вҖІ, ВҜ5)ЛңО“(ВҜ6, ВҜ6вҖІ, 3)
ОЈxc(1, 2) = iW(1, ВҜ4)G(1, ВҜ3)ЛңО“(ВҜ3, 2, ВҜ4)
ВҜПҮ(1, 2) = вҲ’iG(1, ВҜ3)G(ВҜ3вҖІ, 1)ЛңО“(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ, 2)
W(1, 2) = vC(1, 2) + vC(1, ВҜ3вҖІ)ВҜПҮ(ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4)W(ВҜ4, 2)
G(1, 2) = G0(1, 2) + G0(1, 2)ОЈ(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ)G(ВҜ3вҖІ, 2).
(4.80)
The Hedin equations can be solved iteratively for G and the selfВӯenergy ОЈ: the exchangeВӯcorrelation
part of the selfВӯenergy used to calculate G in the fifth of equations (4.80) is the selfВӯenergy calcuВӯ
lated using the same G in the second of the eqs., eq. (4.80). The selfВӯenergy and GreenвҖҷs function
obtained through this process can then be used to solve the BSE for the twoВӯparticle correlation
function 4ПҮ. However, in practical applications it is not possible to find a convergent behavior
in this set of equations without doing simplifications [16]. Therefore we consider the soВӯcalled
GWВӯapproximation.
4.4. BetheВӯSalpeter equation
39
==PAGE:46==
GWВӯapproximation
In order to determine the selfВӯenergy, we often choose to apply the soВӯcalled GWВӯapproximation.
This approximation corresponds to making an initial guess of neglecting the vertex correction by
assuming that [10],
ОҙОЈxc(1, 2)
ОҙG(ВҜ5вҖІ, ВҜ5) = 0,
(4.81)
such that the irreducible vertex, ЛңО“, becomes,
ЛңО“(1, 2, 3) = Оҙ(1, 3)Оҙ(1, 2).
(4.82)
We then iterate the Hedin eqs. (4.80) once, obtaining that
ОЈxc(1, 2) вүҲiW(1, 2)G(1, 2).
(4.83)
Which then leads to,
ОҙОЈxc(1, 2)
ОҙG(1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) = iОҙ(1, 1вҖІ)Оҙ(2, 2вҖІ)W(1, 2) + iG(1, 2) ОҙW(1, 2)
ОҙG(1вҖІ, 2вҖІ).
(4.84)
We decide to neglect the last term in this equation since it is of higher order in W, and we are left
with a BSE kernel Оһ, eq. (4.76), consisting of two terms, one containing the bare and another the
screened interaction,
Оһ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4, ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4вҖІ) вүҲiОҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4вҖІ)Оҙ(ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4)W(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ) вҲ’ivC(ВҜ3, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ4вҖІ+, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ).
(4.85)
To comply with the notation in the literature used in this project [10][16], we have to make a small
redefinition at this point. Recall the relation between the twoВӯparticle correlation function and
the density response function, eq. (4.77), вҲ’4ПҮ(1, 2, 1+, 2+)i = ПҮ(1, 2). To get rid of the вҲ’i we
simply redefine 4ПҮ(1, 2, 1+, 2+) вҶ’вҲ’4ПҮ(1, 2, 1+, 2+)i. We there also redefine 4ПҮnc, eq. (4.74),
4ПҮnc = GG, such that 4ПҮnc вҶ’вҲ’i4ПҮnc. In this way, the BSE in the GWВӯapproximation becomes,
4ПҮ(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ)i =4ПҮnc(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ)i
+4ПҮnc(1, ВҜ3вҖІ, 1вҖІ, ВҜ3)i
 iОҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4вҖІ)Оҙ(ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4)W(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ) вҲ’ivC(ВҜ3, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ4вҖІ+, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ)
 4ПҮ(ВҜ4вҖІ, 2, ВҜ4, 2вҖІ)i,
(4.86)
which simplifies to
4ПҮ(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) = 4ПҮnc(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) = 4ПҮnc(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) + 4ПҮnc(1, ВҜ3вҖІ, 1вҖІ, ВҜ3)Оһ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4, ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4вҖІ)4ПҮ(ВҜ4вҖІ, 2, ВҜ4, 2вҖІ).
(4.87)
And we have redefined the kernel in eq. (4.85),
Оһ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4, ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4вҖІ) = vC(ВҜ3, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ4вҖІ+, ВҜ4)Оҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ) вҲ’W(ВҜ3, ВҜ3вҖІ)Оҙ(ВҜ3, ВҜ4вҖІ)Оҙ(ВҜ3вҖІ, ВҜ4).
(4.88)
The BSE after our reВӯdefinitions therefore retains the same form as in eq. (4.75). The first term
in the kernel corresponds to the RPA and the second term describes a direct interaction between
charged particles, and thus enables us to describe excitonic effects, which will perhaps become
more clear in a moment. If the screened Coulomb interaction W between the electron and hole is
small, corresponding to them being very weakly bound, the second term becomes negligible and
the expression reduces to RPA, as discussed earlier.

The BSE in frequency domain
To shortly sum up some of the previous sections, we would like to determine the twoВӯparticle corВӯ
relation function from the BSE, eq. (4.75). The twoВӯparticle correlation function then describes
the propagation of two particles under the influence of variations in the Hartree potential and the
exchangeВӯcorrelation selfВӯenergy. From the BSE we are able to determine the density response
function since ПҮ(1, 2) = 4ПҮ(1, 2, 1+, 2+), which then holds information about excitonic behavior
in the physical spectra as opposed to the ПҮ(1, 2) that can simply be found using RPA. The electronВӯ
hole interaction is described through a kernel, eq. (4.76), which contains the functional derivative
of the exchangeВӯcorrelation selfВӯenergy with respect to the GreenвҖҷs function, and this is what is
4.4. BetheВӯSalpeter equation
41
==PAGE:48==
different from the RPA where this quantity is neglected. In order to approximate the vertex corВӯ
rection, we use the GW approximation which corresponds to iterating the Hedin equations once
with an initial guess of ОҙОЈ/ОҙG = 0. Now, we would like to reformulate the equation in a way that
enables us to implement and solve it. For this, we need to initially consider the BSE in reciprocal
space.
We make the assumption that the system is time invariant and thus the BSE fulfills,
4ПҮ(t1 + c, t2 + c, t1вҖІ + c, t2вҖІ + c) = 4ПҮ(t1, t2, t1вҖІ, t2вҖІ),
(4.93)
where c is some constant time shift. We omitted the space argument for simplification. There are
multiple ways of defining a set of time arguments that fulfill this criteria and reduce the number of
time arguments. To follow the definitions and conventions of [10], we make the choice,
П„2 = t1 вҲ’t1вҖІ,
П„3 = t2 вҲ’t2вҖІ,
П„1 = 1
2 ((t1 вҲ’t2вҖІ) + (t1вҖІ вҲ’t2)) .
(4.94)
We then define the Fourier transform of the elements of the BSE as,
C(П„1, П„2, П„3) =
1

(4.95)
It is shown in [17] that this leads to the expression for the BSE in reciprocal space,
4ПҮ(Пү1, Пү2, Пү3) = 4ПҮnc(Пү1, Пү2, Пү3) +
4ПҮnc(Пү1, Пү2, ВҜПү4)

(4.96)

П„2 = П„3 = 0,
П„1 = t1 вҲ’t2,
(4.97)

Оһ(Пү1, Пү2, Пү3) = vC вҲ’W(Пү2 вҲ’Пү3).

where we defined Пү4 = Пү2 вҲ’Пү3. Using the definitions in eq. (4.94), the two deltaВӯfunctions in eq.

4.4. BetheВӯSalpeter equation

example where we have 5 valence and 5 conduction bands with 10 kВӯpoints each, such that we have

one of the four momenta in the expression is fixed. In this way we are left with 5Г—1 = 5 eigenstates
(corresponding to the number of v or c bands) for one of the indices. And if we further fix q, we
are left with only two momenta that can vary, and the Hamiltonian has the reduced dimension of
(50 Г— 5)(50 Г— 5). For example if we know k2, q and k3 in the diagrams, we can find k1 and k4.
