4.4. BetheВӯSalpeter equation
==PAGE:55==
Scaling problems
With the BSE on matrix form, eq. (4.137), where the elements of the equation are found by solving
the eigenvalue problem for the BSE Hamiltonian in eq. (4.138), we can find the density response
function in reciprocal space using eq. (4.147).
However, creating the BSE Hamiltonian is computationally costly. The size of the matrix scales
as N2
v N2
c N2
k, where Nv are the number of valence bands, Nc the number of conduction bands and
Nk the number of kВӯpoints, cf. eq. (4.144). We need to determine the kernel Оһ, eq. (4.148) for
every element in this matrix, which quickly become a lot of elements. In particular evaluating the
many matrix elements of W is expensive [10]. vC is diagonal in G which reduces the problem of
determining its matrix elements slightly, but for every of the N2
v N2
c N2
k elements, W needs to be
calculated for all combinations of G and GвҖІ and we also need to determine ОөвҲ’1. The latter will be
found using an RPA calculation. But this means that for every matrix element and combination of
G and GвҖІ, one has to do an RPA calculation to find ОөвҲ’1 and evaluate the brackets of eq. (4.150).
Further, when solving the eigenvalue problem of the BSE Hamiltonian, such that we can construct
the twoВӯparticle correlation function using eq. (4.137), we need to perform a diagonalization. If
many transitions are included, the matrix may become too large and memory problems may occur
[10].
The fact that the BSE is so computationally heavy means that in real calculations we have to reduce
the number of bands that we include in the calculations. In practice, this means that we will only
include a few valence and conduction bands close to the Fermi energy. The absorption spectrum of
a given material depends on the energy differences between the bands, and if we only include some
of the highest valence bands and the lowest conduction bands, we will only obtain the absorption
spectrum at low frequencies, since these transitions are the lowest energy excitations possible for
the material in consideration. The imaginary part of the density response function corresponds
to specific transitions between the bands and it is local in nature, as seen in section 3.3 about the
KramersВӯKronig relation, the peaks having just some small finite broadening. Therefore, unless
a specific frequency transition has sufficient energy to cross over into bands not encompassed in
the BSE calculation, the limited selection of bands incorporated in the BSE will still accurately
yield the absorption spectrum at low frequencies. However, as one may recall from section 3.3,
the real part will depend on the entire spectrum of the imaginary part. Thus, a consequence of only
including a few bands in the calculation is that the real part of the density response function not
will be converged, nor at low frequencies.
TammВӯDancoff approximation
To handle at least some of the scaling problems in the BSE, we can use the TammВӯDancoff apВӯ
proximation. There are two different versions of this approximation. According to [10] and [16]
the TammВӯDancoff approximation involves only including the first and fourth quadrant of the BSE
Hamiltonian (4.138), thus excluding the coupling elements of the matrix. In this way, the matrices
entering the BSE become Hermitian. According to [18] on the other hand, the TammВӯDancoff inВӯ
volves only including the positive frequency transitions, which in our expression for H2p, (4.138),
corresponds to only including the first quadrant in the calculations. We look more into these two
versions of the TammВӯDancoff approximation in chapter 6.
4.5
Summary
LetвҖҷs sum a bit up on what we have learned so far, also from previous chapters. In chapter 3, we saw
how to derive an exact expression for the density response function. We saw that through TDDFT,
it can be connected to the nonВӯinteracting density response function, eq. (3.68), which is a twoВӯ
point Dyson equation for the density response function containing a term with the Hartree potential
and a term with the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel. We then looked into the alternative formulation
of manyВӯbody perturbation theory and saw that the term in TDDFT containing the Hartree potential
4.5. Summary
49
==PAGE:56==
corresponds to the expression we obtain using RPA. The RPA, yielding a mean field expression
for the interaction between charges in the system, contains the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction
and is therefore successful in describing plasmons, but fails in capturing excitonic effects due to
the neglect of vertex corrections. To go further, we introduced and derived the BSE, obtaining a
Dyson equation for the twoВӯparticle correlation function, which is a four point equation that can
be used to obtain the density response function. We then applied the GWВӯapproximation and saw
that the kernel is split in two terms like in TDDFT, the first term once again corresponding to
RPA, but the second term now describing the screened Coulomb interaction between electrons and
holes, thus capturing the excitonic effects. We then discussed the fact that the BSE, being a fourВӯ
point equation, is computationally expensive, and introduced the TammВӯDancoff approximation
to reduce the problem slightly. We have also previously seen that the density response function
obeys the KramersВӯKronig relation, section 3.3. Thus, the real part of the density response function
depends on the entire imaginary part of the function, meaning that we can only assume it to be fully
converged if all optical transitions are included. For real material however, we will not be able to
include enough bands in the BSE calculation for the real part to be converged.
50
4.5. Summary
==PAGE:57==
5
Hybrid Approaches: Creating Hybrid
Expressions for Electric Susceptibility
The BSE calculations will only include few bands around the band gap. This means that we will
only have information about transitions up to small, finite frequencies, but perhaps a greater issue in
regards to this, is that the real part of the density response function will most often not be converged
at low frequencies. The RPA density response function on the other hand can easily be calculated
up to very high frequencies, but does not capture the excitonic effects which, fortunately, mostly
are present at the low frequencies close to the band gap. Motivated by this, we will now search for
hybrid functions that combine the strengths of these methods.
Our first attempt is to take the twoВӯpoint equation from TDDFT and determine an expression for
the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel based on the BSE calculated density response function in order to
capture excitonic interactions. The Hartree term can of course be found by doing an RPA calcuВӯ
lation. We will call this method for determining the density response function the Kernel Hybrid
function. We will see how to determine another hybrid from a diagrammatic derivation also comВӯ
bining RPA and BSE calculations and call this method the Simple Hybrid function. We will show
that the Kernel Hybrid and the Simple Hybrid functions can be connected, thereby enabling us
to interpolate between them. We then consider a more simple approach, where we do an interВӯ
polating transition between the imaginary part of a BSE calculated density response function at
low frequencies and an RPA calculated one at higher frequencies, obtaining the imaginary part of
an approximated combined density response function, which we then Hilbert transform to obtain
the entire approximated density response function. We call this the Transition Вӯ H method, H for
Hilbert. And we finally also consider a variant of the Simple Hybrid where we only create the hyВӯ
brid from the imaginary parts of the RPA and BSE density response functions and then do a Hilbert
transform to find the real part. We call this the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H method. We explain the work of
[2] in order to be able to compare our results to their method. Here they made an exponential tail on
the imaginary part of the BSE density response function in order to impose the fВӯsum rule, i.e. the
area of the tail should correspond to that of the transitions not included in the BSE calculation, and
this imaginary part was then Hilbert transformed. We call this the fВӯsum method. First however,
we will introduce the concept of finite density response functions, since all hybrid functions make
use of this concept.
5.1
Finite density response functions
In principle, the BSE and RPA calculated density response functions should include infinitely many
bands. That is of course not possible, and for practical applications not necessary either. Instead,
having a finite but converged number of bands will suffice. While this is achievable for the RPA, the
computational cost of the BSE unfortunately makes convergence in the number of bands unattainВӯ
able. Therefore all hybrid functions will in some way make use the concept of вҖҷfiniteвҖҷ density
response function in the sense that not a converged number of bands are included in the calculation
of them. They will also depend on a fully converged RPA, which then in our terminology is not
finite. Thus, we introduce a subscript, F, for finite on the density response functions. Finding the
finite BSE calculated density response function, ПҮBSE
F
, then involves selecting a computationally
realistic number of bands to be included, while at the same time taking into account that we would
like to include enough bands for the most prominent excitonic effects to be captured. A reasonable
rule of thumb turns out be to include bands within about a cutoff of Оҙ = 2 eV on each side of
the band gap, вҲҶEgap. This means, that we are ensured that all transitions in the frequency range
Пүc = EF Вұ
 вҲҶEgap/2 + Оҙ

are included, and the BSE calculation should describe the correct
Chapter 5. Hybrid Approaches: Creating Hybrid Expressions for Electric Susceptibility
51
==PAGE:58==
spectrum including excitonic peaks at these low frequencies up until this cutoff. After this point,
ideally the fully converged RPA calculation should contain the correct spectrum. There will also
be some transitions from the BSE calculation up until the maximal q = 0 energy difference beВӯ
tween the lowest included valence band and the highest included conduction band, we can call this
frequency point Пүm where m stands for maximum. The concept is illustrated in figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Band structure of bulk rutile TiO2. The red lines represent the cutoff limit. The blue
bands are the ones included in the calculation, while the green bands are not. It is hard to see why
the lowest included band falls within the cutoff, so we have marked the point where it is on the
inclusion side of the cutoff limit.
Figure 5.1 illustrates how we select the number of bands included in ПҮBSE
F
. The red curves mark
the range of included energies. This leads to the blue bands being included, while the green bands
are not. We have marked the direct band gap with a red arrow to the right, which according to the
DFTВӯPBE calculation is 1.756 eV and located in the О“Вӯpoint. We then include bands within 2.0 eV
to each side of this direct band gap. This leads to 6 valence bands and 6 conduction bands being
included in the calculation, all marked with numbers at the right hand side of the figure. As the
figure illustrates, the bands just need to be within the range of included energies at one point to be
included in the calculations. Thus, the sixth valence band is just included since it crosses the red
line at the point marked with a black circle.
Some hybrids make use of a finite RPA calculation, ПҮRPA
F
, as well. In which ways will become
clearer in the next sections. But the investigations in this project revealed that the ПҮRPA
F
calculaВӯ
tions, although they should include the same bands as included in the ПҮBSE
F
calculation, should be
converged with respect to the number of kВӯpoints, even when it was not possible to obtain full kВӯ
points convergence in the ПҮBSE
F
calculation due to memory problems. The peaks and irregularities
of the ПҮRPA
F
calculations, present solely due to a lack of kВӯpoints, will be reflected in the hybrid denВӯ
sity response functions, i.e. they will not be removed by whatever operation that is done to create
the hybrid. And since these irregularities or peaks are only present due to a lack of convergence,
they are of course very unwanted. When the BSE calculations are not fully kВӯpoint converged,
they too are expected to contain convergence related peaks and irregularities, but it is not so that
the errors in the ПҮRPA
F
and ПҮBSE
F
will cancel each other. Thus, to minimize the amount of unwanted
behavior in the hybrid density response functions, we restrict the convergence issues to be in the
52
5.1. Finite density response functions
==PAGE:59==
ПҮBSE
F
calculations. You can find more information on the actual calculations and convergence in
appendix I.
5.2
The Kernel Hybrid
In the beginning of this thesis we studied linear response theory, and we derived the density reВӯ
sponse function using the formalism of TDDFT. Thus, an obvious way of avoiding using the fourВӯ
point BSE is using the density response function from TDDFT since it is just a twoВӯpoint equation.
The formulation in TDDFT is thus simpler than that of BSE although the general expressions, eq.
(3.68) and (4.75) are constructed very similarly with the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel fxc being
analogous to the functional derivative of the selfВӯenergy with respect to the GreenвҖҷs function in
the BSE. However, the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel fxc is unknown, whereas we saw that we can
approximate the selfВӯenergy in the GWВӯapproximation. So even though it is easier to solve the
twoВӯargument TDDFT equation than the fourВӯargument BSE, we have to work our way around not
knowing the kernel.
The expression used in TDDFT in reciprocal space was given in eq. (3.68) and is restated here for
convenience, but without the function arguments q, Пү to save space,

(5.14)
The function should be understood in the following way: up until the frequencies corresponding
to the bands included in the finite calculations, that is within Пүm, the kernel is different from zero,
and after this point, it becomes zero. However, remember that we are only sure that all transitions
are included up until Пүc. If we are above the cutoff point, the Kernel Hybrid function yields the
full ПҮRPA density response function, but if we are below, the kernel introduced a correction to the
full RPA calculation.
Due to the construction of the kernel, eq. (5.12), and the way it appears in the overall expression for
the hybrid density response function, eq. (5.14), it is very sensitive to small differences between
the finite RPA and BSE calculation at larger energies, where only few transitions are present: the
one minus the kernel is inverted, and the finite RPA and BSE calculations are both inverted to
construct the kernel. So a small value for the density response functions at a given frequency in
the imaginary parts of the RPA and BSE calculations can result in a large kernel if the two values are
not the exact same. And the large kernel then results in a small hybrid density response function.
At energies above Пүc, the imaginary parts of ПҮBSE
F
and ПҮRPA
F
become gradually smaller since fewer
and fewer transitions occur with still larger frequencies, up until Пүm where we reach the energy
difference between the lowest included valence band and the highest included conduction band,
after which they should both become zero. But the single transitions occurring between Пүc and Пүm
are not located at the exact same frequencies in the two calculations. That the transitions are not
located at the exact same frequency is less of a problem at smaller energies, where the transitions
are part of a continuum. Having the dynamics in the overall expression as just described, even a
small difference in the calculations can make the BSE and RPA calculation dominate the hybrid
density response function, dragging it down to 0 in the range between Пүc and Пүm instead of having
it approaching the full RPA calculation as desired. Therefore, we decided to multiply the kernel
with a FermiВӯDirac function, which then kills the kernel at frequencies above the cutoff frequency,
Пүc. After this point, the kernel is killed over a range which we decided to be 1/2 eV, i.e. we define
a transition temperature ЛңT, such that kB ЛңT = 1/2 eV, and the hybrid function becomes the full RPA
calculation. Thus, our expression for the Kernel Hybrid function is,
ПҮKH

X

5.3
The Simple Hybrid
The Simple Hybrid function has its basis in a more intuitive argument. Recall the BSE in the
GWВӯapproximation, e.g. in its diagrammatic form, after imposing the condition enabling us to
obtain the density response function from 4ПҮ(1, 2, 1вҖІ, 2вҖІ) with 1вҖІ = 1+ and 2вҖІ = 2+, restated here
convenience,

Imagine iterating the middle diagram. It is clear, that we would obtain a pair bubble, connected
with a bare Coulomb interaction to a pair bubble, and so on. Thus what is the apparent is that we
would obtain the RPA expression for the density response function, eq. (4.52),
вҲ’ПҮRPA =
+
+
+ ...
(5.18)
And then we would also obtain diagrams with at least one screened Coulomb interaction line.
At each vertex we have a sum over all states. See for example the expression for the pair bubble,
eq. (3.34). Thus, the finite BSE and RPA calculations will have fewer included bands and therefore
fewer states in this sum compared to the fully converged RPA calculations where the sum at the
vertex ideally is over all interband transitions. The idea in the Simple Hybrid function is then to
take the BSE calculation, subtract the finite RPA calculation, i.e. removing the diagrams in (5.18)
from (5.17) with only a finite number of transitions, and then replace them by the fully converged
RPA calculation,

5.4
Connection between the Kernel Hybrid and Simple Hybrid
In this section we show that there is a straightforward connection between the Kernel and Simple
Hybrid functions. More specifically, we show that by incorporating a complex number x in the
Kernel Hybrid, it converges to the Simple Hybrid in the limit of large x. The connection has not
been used in actual calculations of hybrids for the materials in consideration in this project, as
we will see in the next chapter. We derive the connection anyway because it is interesting that
such a connection can be created, but also because one could imagine that a possible alternative
hybrid approach could be created from a combination of the Kernel and Simple Hybrid functions,
using a nonВӯextremal value for x. If that was to be the case, and if it was to be useful, we should
create a criteria for finding an appropriate value for x. That could e.g. be an x yielding the correct
fВӯsum. However, none of the materials considered in this project seemed to benefit from such a
combined hybrid function, since Вӯ as we will later see Вӯ in all cases, either the Kernel or Simple
hybrid functions will in themselves yield satisfying predictions. However, the investigations in this
project have been strongly limited by time and thus we have only considered in total 4 different
materials. Therefore, we cannot rule out the possibility that such an approach could be beneficial
more generally.
Consider the Kernel Hybrid function in the G = GвҖІ = 0 version, eq. (5.16),

5.6
The Transition Вӯ H
Finally, we have the Transition Вӯ H function. This is the simplest of the methods and does not use
a finite RPA calculation. In this method we naively transition through interpolation from the BSE
imaginary part to the full RPA imaginary part and find the real part through a Hilbert transform.
The transition is made over the course of 2 eV in the following way. The starting point is Пүc, and
we then find two frequency points at 1 eV to each side of Пүc, Пү1 = Пүc вҲ’1eV and Пү2 = Пүc + 1eV.
Since these two points are not necessarily on our frequency grid, we more specifically find the two
points on the grid closest to Пү1 and Пү2 respectively. We then create a function x with numbers

5.7
The fВӯsum method
The fВӯsum method was proposed by [2] and for some of the materials we will be comparing the
hybrid functions to this approach. The fВӯsum has its basis in the soВӯcalled oscillator strengths fij
which was originally introduced in a classical treatment of the absorption and emission processes.
Absorption and emission were originally treated in a dipole oscillator model, and the oscillator
strength was used to explain why some transition lines are stronger than other. With quantum
mechanics we now know that the oscillator strength has its connection to dipole matrix elements
[4],
fij = 2mПүji
3
|вҹЁj|r|iвҹ©|2,
(5.32)
where Пүji = Оөj вҲ’Оөi is the energy difference of the two states between which the transitions
happens. According to the ThomasВӯReicheВӯKuhn sum rule, or the fВӯsum rule, stating the sum of
oscillator strengths is equal to the number of electrons in the system,
X
ij
fij = ne.
(5.33)
Thus, the oscillator strength is a conserved quantity for a given system in consideration. Eq. (5.33)
can be rewritten to give us the relation [2],
Z вҲһ
0
Пү Im ПҮ(Пү)dПү = ПҖnee2
2m .
(5.34)
For the relation to be satisfied, the density response function needs however to be converged.
Otherwise we lack the highВӯfrequency transitions that would otherwise have contributed to the
leftВӯhand side of this expression and the relation will not be satisfied. The fВӯsum method proposed
by [2] exploits this. The idea is to use the full, wellВӯconverged RPA calculation to find the fВӯsum.
We then calculate the leftВӯhand side of eq. (5.34) with the BSE density response function and ask
ourselves the question of how much is missing in order to obtain the true fВӯsum. We then add an
exponential tail to the imaginary part of BSE calculated density response function and fit the slope
Оі to obtain the right fВӯsum [2],
Z Пүc
0
Пү Im ПҮBSE
F
(Пү)dПү +

Пүc

Z вҲһ
0
Пү Im ПҮRPA(Пү)dПү.
(5.35)
The parameter C0 is fitted to ensure continuity between the exponential tail and the imaginary part
of the BSE density response function.
The real part of this modified imaginary part of a density response function is then found with a
Hilbert transform.

6
The TammВӯDancoff Approximation
In general we have to use the TammВӯDancoff approximation (TDA) in order to do BSE calculations
due to the scaling problems. In this chapter we consider how the TDA changes the results and how
to improve the results under this limitation. To evaluate, we consider bulk silicon in a FCC lattice.
The simplicity of the material means that the real part at low frequencies is converged at just
8 bands, which for Si corresponds to 4 valence bands and 4 conduction bands to each side of the
band gap. The convergence test is shown in appendix I.0.5. Thus, contrary to most other materials,
we are actually able to perform a BSE calculation with enough bands included for the real part of
the polarizability function to be well converged. And the few bands needed for convergence also
means that we can do the BSE calculations without the TDA such that we can evaluate on the
inclusion of this approximation. Details on the calculation settings can be found in section I.
6.1
Influence of the TDA on the real and imaginary part of
polarizability for silicon
In the GPAW code, it is possible to perform an RPA calculation with the BSE code, i.e. to put
W = 0 in the BSE calculations. By doing so, we can perform two calculations on Si and with the
same parameters: one calculation using the BSE code in RPA mode, and one using the RPA code
to see that the two codes are in agreement. Initially, we turn off the TDA in the BSE calculation to
confirm that the two codes are in agreement. Afterwards, we turn on the TDA, still performing an
RPA calculation with the BSE code. By comparing the real and imaginary parts of the polarizability
from the BSE and RPA codes, we see how introducing the TDA changes the results. We also do
a Hilbert transform of the imaginary part of the polarizability found with the BSE code under the
constraint that the G = GвҖІ = 0 component of the polarizability is an antiВӯsymmetric function,
to see if the KramersВӯKronig relation is satisfied. We denote this function BSEH. The results are
shown in figure 6.1. In figure 6.1 (B) is shown the imaginary part of the polarizability. The RPA
A
B
Figure 6.1: Three RPA calculation on Si. The red curve is normal RPA, the green curve is found
with the BSE code in RPA mode without the TDA, and the orange curve shows the effect of includВӯ
ing the TDA on this material. The imaginary parts are in agreement (B), but including the TDA
lowers the real part (A). The blue curve in (A) shows the Hilbert transform of the imaginary part of
the polarizability found with the BSE code in RPA mode under the TDA and under the constraint
that it is antiВӯsymmetric.
calculation (red), the BSE calculation in RPA mode with (orange) and without (green) the TDA are
close to being in agreement, even though including the TDA does lift the peaks slightly around 3.7
Chapter 6. The TammВӯDancoff Approximation
61
==PAGE:68==
and 4.5 eV. In figure 6.1 (A), displaying the real part of the polarizability, we see that once again
the RPA calculation (red) and the BSE calculation in RPA mode without the TDA (green) are in
full agreement. However, this time, when including the TDA (orange), we obtain a result that is
off compared the RPA calculation. And what is even worse is that if we do a Hilbert transform
of the imaginary part of the polarizability found with the BSE code in RPA mode under the TDA,
figure 6.1 (B, orange), under the constraint that it is an odd function, we obtain a different real part
than the one obtained from the code, figure 6.1 (A, blue). Thus, the polarizability resulting from
doing a BSE calculation under the TDA does not obey KramersВӯKronig when we take into account
that the polarizability is antiВӯsymmetric. This is very problematic.
It turns out, the majority of the problems, at least for Si, stems from the GPAW version of the TDA
not taking into account that the polarizability is an odd function. This is illustrated in figure 6.2.
A
D
C
B
Figure 6.2: The four plots are meant to illustrate the issue of using the GPAW version of the TDA,
where only the first quadrant of the BSE matrix is included. In (A) we have extracted the numeraВӯ
tor of the polarizability in the Lehmann representation, called вҖҷweightsвҖҷ, and the eigenvalues, and
manually calculated the polarizability using the formula for the polarizability in the Lehmann repВӯ
resentation, confirming that the imaginary part of the polarizability is an odd function. We have
also plotted the imaginary part of the polarizability found with the BSE code in RPA mode. In (C)
we shown the real part of the same calculations. In (C) we also show the Hilbert transform of the
imaginary part of the polarizability that was manually calculated from the weights and eigenvalues
to confirm, that the polarizability obeys the KramersВӯKronig. Figure (B) shows the same calculaВӯ
tion, but now only with positive frequencies. (D) then shows that now all three calculations are in
agreement also in the real part.
In the data behind figure 6.2 we have extracted the eigenvalues and weights, that is the numeraВӯ
tor of the polarizability in the Lehmann representation, found in the BSE code and put them into
eq. (3.15). This yields the red curves in (A) and (C), and we have also plotted the polarizability
obtained from the BSE code, the green curves. In (C), we see that the real part from eq. (3.15)
does indeed obey the KramersВӯKronig relation (red, blue), while the BSE code has resulted in a
real part (green) that is not in agreement with the two others, even though the positive frequency
transitions were in agreement as seen in (A). If we restrict ourselves to only consider the imaginary
62
6.1. Influence of the TDA on the real and imaginary part of polarizability for silicon
==PAGE:69==
part in the positive frequency range (B), also in the eq. (3.15), we see that the real part calculated
with the equation, the one found with a Hilbert transformation of the imaginary part and the real
part obtained from the BSE code are all in agreement. This confirms that the main problem in the
BSE code is that the negative frequency transitions are not included. At this point, this may seem
to be a trivial conclusion, but at the time of the investigations there were some different possible
explanations for the lower lying real part of the BSE outputted polarizabilities. It was possible that
the BSE code behind the scene did take into account the negative frequency transitions although
they were not outputted. It was also possible that the problem was really in the calculated spectra,
i.e. in the imaginary part. In this connection, it could also be a more general consequence of the
TDA. Neglecting not only the negative frequency transitions but also the offВӯdiagonal blocks in the
BSE Hamiltonian, might influence both the imaginary and real part of the polarizability, making
the lower lying real part unavoidable when using the TDA. After all, the imaginary part of the
polarizability found with the BSE code, figure 6.1 (B), is slightly different from the RPA calcuВӯ
lated one. However, what the investigations show is that by imposing the fact that the imaginary
part of the polarizability is antiВӯsymmetric and doing a Hilbert transform under this constraint of
the outputted imaginary part from the BSE subject to the TDA, we may improve our predictions
significantly. And the offВӯdiagonal blocks of the matrix do not influence the spectrum that much,
at least for Si.
What we have shown is that the GPAW BSE code ignores the antiВӯsymmetric nature of the G =
GвҖІ = 0 component of the polarizability, and that the results are significantly improved by either
Hilbert transforming the imaginary part of the polarizability obtained from the BSE code under
this constraint or by simply including the negative frequency transitions directly in the BSE code.
To reduce the computational complexity when creating the BSE matrix, it makes sense to use the
GPAW version of the the TDA, where we only include the positive frequency transitions, instead
of the versions of the TDA proposed by [10] and [16]. But afterwards, we should add the negative
frequency transitions to impose the antiВӯsymmetry of the function when determining the polarВӯ
izability. Throughout the rest of this thesis, we have therefore made this correction. The early
generated results are based on a Hilbert transform of the imaginary part, while some of the later
results are obtained after an implementation of the negative frequency transitions in the BSE code
explicitly, thus directly calculating a real part that obeys the KramersВӯKronig removing the need
for a Hilbert transform after the calculation. The two approaches are equivalent, but the Hilbert
transform is sensitive to the imaginary part of the polarizability going to zero in both ends of the
spectra and the best results are obtained after appending a lot of extra zeros in both ends. This was
therefore something that we did, and if the imaginary part did not go completely to zero at zero
frequency, we subtracted the value of the polarizability at zero frequency before doing the Hilbert
transform. However, as figure 6.2 (C) also shows, doing a Hilbert transform of the imaginary part
is in general just as good as including the negative frequency transitions explicitly.
After making this correction, we calculate the refractive index and the extinction coefficient for
Si and compare to experimental data, now putting the BSE calculation in BSE mode. What we
further want to point out here is the importance of shifting the eigenenergies in calculations of the
polarizability, such that the band gap of the calculations matches the true band gap of the material
in consideration. In general, DFT tends to underestimate the band gap. And the real part of the
polarizability, especially in the limits of very low or very large frequency, is sensitive to the peaks
being located correctly. Thus, before finding the real part with a Hilbert transform, the peaks in the
imaginary part of the spectrum need to be shifted to match the band gap. This is simply done by
adding an energy to the frequencies that makes the peaks match the band gap and then appending
a tail of zeroes to fill out the gap that has then appeared between the calculated polarizability and
zero energy. We denote the BSE calculated polarizability in which the real part is found using a
Hilbert transform and where the imaginary part has been shifted to match the band gap, BSEHS.
6.1. Influence of the TDA on the real and imaginary part of polarizability for silicon
63
==PAGE:70==
The results are shown in figure 6.3.
A
B
Figure 6.3: The plot (A) shows the refractive index of Si, while the plot (B) shows the extinction
coefficient. The black dots are experimental data obtained from [19]. The red curve is based on the
BSE calculation of the polarizability that was originally obtained from [2] which is an output of the
BSE code. The green curve shows the effect of doing a Hilbert transform of the imaginary part to
find the real part of the density response, before using it to calculate the refractive index/extinction
coefficient. Note that after having found the refractive index, this green curve is moved to match
the band gap. The orange curve shows the effect of energy shifting the eigenvalues and zero
appending like describe above before doing the Hilbert transform. Note the striking agreement
with the experimental data.
In conclusion, the TDA generates an imaginary part of the polarizability at positive frequencies
that, for silicon at least, is in satisfying agreement with the polarizability calculated without any apВӯ
proximations. However, the real part is underestimated, primarily due to the negative frequencies
not being included. So to improve our predictions, before creating any hybrid functions, we need
to include the negative frequencies or Hilbert transform the imaginary part under the constraint
that the polarizability is an odd function. Further, we need to correct the imaginary parts of the
BSE and RPA calculations to match the correct band gap. Due to the shift of energies, the RPA
calculations will also need to be Hilbert transformed such that the KramersВӯKronig relation is still
obeyed. A last point to be made is that we in general do not know the band gap of the materials
in consideration. More accurate calculations are available Вӯ like GWВӯcalculations Вӯ but predicting
correct band gaps are out of the scope of this project. Thus, since we are benchmarking on exВӯ
perimental data, we match the location of the excitonic peaks in the calculated BSE spectrum to
the excitonic peak in the experimental datasets by shifting the frequencies as just described before
creating the hybrids.

Figure 6.5: The left figure displays the absolute values of the BSE Hamiltonian that is used to create
the density response function and the right figure displays the BSE Hamiltonian used to create the
polarizability. I.e. the left figure has the longВӯrange interactions included, whereas the right figure
has not. Both matrices has traces and max values that are in the same order of magnitude, and
therefore comparing them on the same scale seems reasonable. The values are colorВӯcoded as
shown at the bar to the right of the figure: the darker the green, the larger the value of the element
inside the BSE matrix.
From figure 6.5 we see that when the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction is included, which it is in
the case of the density response function, the elements in the offВӯdiagonal are more prominent then
in the case where the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction is put to zero. This indicates the TDA will
exclude more information when used in a calculation of the density response function, and is a first
step in the direction of understanding what goes wrong in figure 6.4 when we try to calculate the
density response function with the BSE code under the TDA.
Finally, since the investigation suggests that the TDA fails when the longВӯrange Coulomb interacВӯ
tion is included, we would expect that calculating EELS spectra under the TDA does not go well,
since EELS spectra reveal plasmons which are due to the this longВӯrange interaction. Thus, in figВӯ
ure 6.6, we show an EELS spectrum based on an RPA calculated polarizability on Si together with
an EELS spectrum obtained from the polarizability calculated with the BSE code in RPA mode
under the TDA.
Figure 6.6: Calculated EELS spectrum for Si. The green dashed curve shows an RPA calculation
while the red curve is an BSE calculation with W = 0 thus yielding RPA. We see the effect of
including the TDA since this is the only difference between the calculations.
66
6.2. The longВӯrange Coulomb interaction
==PAGE:73==
In figure 6.6 we see that including the TDA in the calculation yields a result for the EELS spectrum
quite different from the full RPA calculated one. In general, the RPA is good at predicting EELS
spectra, so including the TDA introduces an unwanted change to the predicted EELS spectrum.
Our investigations seem to suggest that the TDA works less well when the longВӯrange Coulomb
interaction is included. This is backed by [10]. Further, we think it does make some intuitive sense:
the offВӯdiagonal blocks of the BSE matrix consist of the coupling part alone, eq. (4.141), i.e. they
hold some of the information about interactions in the system. Neglecting them is in general a
good approximation if matrix elements holding the interactions are small compared to the transiВӯ
tion energies [10]. However, if the longВӯrange Coulomb interactions are taken into account, the
interactions in the system are stronger, and thus the TDA is worse. Thus, in conclusion, we should
use the BSE code to calculate polarizabilities rather than density response functions. Luckily, all
equations that we have derived for the density response function, including the hybrids, are also
valid for the polarizability.
6.2. The longВӯrange Coulomb interaction
67
==PAGE:74==
68
6.2. The longВӯrange Coulomb interaction
==PAGE:75==
7
Applying Hybrid Approaches: Calculating
Electric Susceptibility and Spectra for Solids
In this chapter we present the primary results of this project. We have calculated the polarizability
with RPA, BSE and the hybrid functions described in chapter 5 for the materials TiO2 in rutile
and anatase form, bulk MoS2, hBN and HfS2, and from the polarizability we have determined the
refractive index and the EELS spectrum, sometimes also the extinction coefficient. When available
online, we have included experimental data from various research groups in the plots, to see how
well the hybrids can predict this data. We will be comparing the hybrids and their performance on
predicting the experimental data, first on each of the materials, and later we will see if we can draw
some conclusions across the materials.
7.1
Hybrid model calculations for various solids
Rutile and anatase TiO2 are two semiconductors which have gained interest due to their possible
use in photochemical applications [20, 21], that could be as photocatalysists or in soВӯcalled wet
solar cells [20]. Rutile is the stable form of TiO2 whereas anatase is metastable, but the anatase
form shows the most photocatalytic activity [22]. According to [23] they have the same point
group, being tetragonal, 4/mmm, but they have different structures. We begin by considering the
rutile form. The calculations was done using some experimental lattice parameters of rutile TiO2
that are a = 4.584 Г… and c = 2.953 Г… obtained from [23]. The space group is P42/mnm [24].
As we also illustrated in figure 5.1, we include bands within Оҙ = 2 eV of each side of the band
gap corresponding to 6 valence bands and 6 conduction bands in the finite calculations. The direct
band gap was found by fitting the outputted BSE polarizability to the experimental data, and was
found to be about 3.4 eV. Thus, for this system, Пүc = 3.4eV + 2eV = 5.4eV. More details on the
structure, calculation settings and convergence of the full and finite RPA calculations can be found
in appendix I. We have studied this material with two settings for the polarization of the incoming
electrical field. First we consider the case where the electrical field is polarized along the soВӯcalled
ordinary axis (вҖҷoВӯaxisвҖҷ), meaning that it is parallel to the a/b axis of the unit cell [25]. In figure 7.1
we show the results on our predictions for the refractive index, extinction coefficient and EELS
together with experimental data.
Figure 7.1 shows in (A) and (D) the refractive index and extinction coefficient respectively obtained
directly from the full and finite RPA calculations and the BSE calculation. The black dots are
experimental data. What is apparent Вӯ and perhaps not so surprising Вӯ is that the RPA calculations do
not capture the excitonic peak located just below 4 eV in the refractive index. The BSE calculation
does, but the height of the peak and also what seem to be the static refractive index according
to the experimental data, is underestimated due to the number of bands not being converged, as
described in section 3.3 and 4.4. We do not have experimental data down to 0 eV, but by considering
the tendency of the data, it seems to be aiming at a static refractive index of about 2.5Вӯ2.6, which
is perhaps a bit more than what the fully converged RPA calculation suggests. That the full RPA
calculation seems to underestimate the static refractive index may be due to the influence of the
excitonic peak. The plots (B) and (E) show the refractive index and extinction coefficient found
with the four hybrids, once again together with experimental data. All hybrids seem to capture the
excitons. But the Kernel Hybrid largely overestimates the height of the peaks, and in the refractive
index, it locates of the peak at a frequency that is too small. We will return to why that is in section
7.3. The Simple Hybrid, the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H and Transition Вӯ H are largely in agreement and
predict the experimental data very well. The Simple Hybrid predicts the static refractive index
to be a little smaller than the two Вӯ H functions, but since the Вӯ H functions lie a bit above the

experimental data at low frequencies while the Simple Hybrid lies a bit below, we cannot tell

about 6% larger than that of the band gap corrected RPA, whereas the other hybrids only are about

Considering figure 7.2, the RPA calculations shown in (A,D) do not capture the excitonic effects
present slightly below 4 eV in the refractive index and at about 4.5 eV in the extinction coefficient.
The BSE does capture this effect, but underestimates the height of the peak in the refractive index.
The BSE also underestimates the static refractive index, that the experimental data seems to be
aiming at, which is perhaps around 2.5, in agreement with the static refractive index that the fully
converged RPA calculation suggests. The BSE does however make a very good prediction for
the extinction coefficient. As for the hybrid functions, (B,E), the Simple Hybrid and the two ВӯH
hybrids underestimate the height of the excitonic peak. The ВӯH hybrids seem to perform better at
lower frequencies, yielding what seems to become the correct static refractive index according to
the experimental data. The Kernel Hybrid performs well on this material, predicting both the height
of the excitonic peak and the static refractive index quite in accordance with the experimental data.
The fВӯsum method once again overestimates the static refractive index, as shown in (C). In (F) we
were not able to find experimental EELS for TiO2 explicitly referring to the eВӯaxis. Assuming that
the RPA predicts this spectrum well, all four hybrid functions do however lie on top of the RPA
calculation, and thus all hybrids behave as desired.

We now consider anatase TiO2. The lattice parameters used in the calculations are found experВӯ

Пүc = 4.6eV + 1.6eV = 6.2eV. More details on the structure, calculation settings and and converВӯ

The RPA and BSE calculated refractive index and extinction coefficients for anatase TiO2 are
shown in figure 7.3, (A,D). The RPA calculations do not capture the excitonic peaks seen between
about 3Вӯ5 eV. It does seem like the experimental data on the refractive index is aiming at a static
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids
73
==PAGE:80==
refractive index of just a bit more than 2, perhaps 2.1Вӯ2.2, which is in agreement with the fully
converged RPA. The BSE does capture the excitonic effects, but in the refractive index, the single
peak at about 4.5 eV is split into two peaks in the BSE calculation, and the size of the peaks in the
extinction coefficient are overestimated. This is likely due to convergence issues, as also discussed
in (I.0.2). Many bands have to be included in the BSE calculation to just obtain excitonic effects
up to Оҙ = 1.6 eV above the direct band gap, and the price is fewer kВӯpoints. We did try to decrease
Оҙ but the excitonic peaks were not properly captured with such a small cutoff. More specifically,
we tried a cutoff of 1 eV, and even though the peaks were still there, they were just too small in the
BSE calculation for the hybrids to yield satisfying results. The hybrids need the BSE calculation
to have properly described the excitonic effects, and this need was just not met at a Оҙ below 1.6 eV.
This may at first seem strange: our cutoff energy Пүc with a Оҙ of 1.6 was 6.2 eV, and the excitonic
peaks lie between 3Вӯ5 eV. So one could ask, why we cannot have a cutoff energy down to 5 eV and
still capture all excitonic effects. Here we need to remember that the Coulomb attraction between
the electron and hole lowers the energy compared to the energy difference between the valence
and conduction band that the transition happened between. Thus, even though the two bands have
an energy difference above approximately 5 eV, they may contribute with an exciton in the energy
range of 3Вӯ5 eV. Now, the hybrid functions are shown in (B,E) for the refractive index and extinction
coefficient respectively. The Simple Hybrid and the two Вӯ H hybrids seem to capture both the height
of the excitonic peaks quite well as well as the static refractive index. They do however inherit the
issues from the BSE calculation, having too large peaks in the extinction coefficients and having
the single excitonic peak at about 4.5 eV in the refractive index being split into two peaks. The
peaks in the extinction coefficient are a bit smaller for these hybrids compared to the BSE due to
the RPA calculations. The Kernel Hybrid does not perform well on this material, largely overВӯ
and underestimating the peaks in the refractive index around 3.5 eV and 4.5 eV respectively. Also
the extinction coefficient is very off. The fВӯsum method (C) captures the excitonic peaks in the
refractive index quite well, but seems to overestimate the static refractive index. In (F) is shown
the EELS spectrum. Once again, all hybrids lie on top of the RPA calculated EELS, which captures
the overall trends in the experimental spectrum with plasmonic peaks around 15 and 28 eV. Note
that we have a smaller peak in the experimental data in the range of around 5Вӯ8 eV. The RPA does
not fully capture this peak, having only a small peak at around 8 eV, whereas the hybrids actually
show more activity in this frequency range. The peak, being low in energy, is likely influenced by
excitons, which the RPA do not describe, making the hybrids predict the experimental data slightly
better here. One should take into account that EELS spectra are less well predicted under the TDA,
as discussed in chapter 6. And the excitonic effects in the EELS spectrum above are introduced by
the BSE calculation which is subject to the TDA. However, this was due to the TDA working less
well when the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction is included. And if the peak in the EELS spectrum
is excitonic in nature, then it is not only caused by the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction. Besides,
as seen back at figure 6.6, the TDA makes us predict the plasmonic peak less well, but the peak
has not vanished.
Next we consider bulk MoS2 which too is a semiconductor, and further is part of a class of layered
materials called transition metal dichalcogenides. Typically these are composed of hexagonally
packed layers, which consist of a transition metal, in the case of MoS2 that is molybdenum, surВӯ
rounded by two layers of a chalcogen, which in the case of MoS2 is sulfur, in a trigonal prismatic
arrangement [27]. Van der Waals forces bind adjacent layers together. Transition metal dichalcoВӯ
genides have gained attention due to this van der Waals anisotropic nature yielding some interesting
optical features [6]. E.g. MoS2 has a large birefringence, which is the phenomenon that an incomВӯ
ing ray of light can be split in two when it passes through the material such that the two rays takes
different paths. This is due to the material having different refractive indices along different crystal
axes, such than an incoming ray of light, being polarized along these different axes, propagates at
different speeds [28]. Thus, bulk MoS2 can e.g. be used as a polarizer. As another example MoS2
74
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids
==PAGE:81==
has been used to create fieldВӯeffect transistors [27]. The lattice parameters of bulk 2H вҲ’MoS2,
which is the most stable phase of bulk MoS2 and refers to the trigonal prismatic arrangement of the
chalcogen, are found experimentally in [29] to be a = b = 3.161 Г… and c = 12.295 Г…. The space
group is P63/mmc [24]. For MoS2 we were able to include bands with Оҙ = 2.5 eV on each side
of the band gap corresponding to 4 valence bands and 8 conduction bands. The direct band gap
was found to be about 2 eV by fitting the results to the experimental data. Thus, for this system,
Пүc = 2eV + 2.5eV = 4.5eV. More details on the structure, calculation settings and and converВӯ
gence tests can be found in appendix I, section I.0.3. The results of our predictions of the refractive
index, extinction coefficient and EELS together with experimental data are shown in figure 7.4.

In figure 7.4 (A,D) we see the refractive index and extinction coefficient respectively obtained
from the two RPA calculations and the BSE calculation together with some experimental data.
Note that the experimental data is not a direct measurement of the refractive index / extinction
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids

==PAGE:82==
coefficient. Instead [6] has measured the dielectric function from which we have calculated the
refractive index and extinction coefficients from eq. (2.15) and (2.16). This is just to be consistent
since we have in general been considering the refractive index and extinction coefficients for the
other materials. The experimental data displays two very sharp excitonic peaks just below 2 eV
which the BSE only partially captures. The peaks are there, but they are too wide and not high
enough. Further, the second peak is not located close enough to the first peak compared to the
experimental data. To capture the sharpness of these peaks, it is likely that we would need many
more kВӯpoints and a smaller broadening О· in our calculations. However, we were unable to include
more kВӯpoints in the BSE calculation due to memory limitations, and having a smaller О· without
increasing the number of kВӯpoints would not yield satisfying results in the rest of the spectra. The
two RPA calculations do not capture the excitonic effects, but we do see a rise in the spectrum
at the band gap around the 2 eV, this rise being located close the second peak, and thus likely
also contributing to this peak, which is then not solely excitonic in nature. The experimental data
seems to suggest a static refractive index just below 4, perhaps 3.8Вӯ3.9. To no surprise at this point,
the BSE predicts a static refractive index too small compared to this. Perhaps more surprisingly,
the fully converged RPA calculation only suggests a static refractive index of approximately 3.5.
There are two possible reasons this. Either the RPA calculation is not well enough converged,
or the excitonic peaks cause an increase in the static refractive index. This latter reason is not
unlikely since the excitonic peaks are quite large and located closely to 0 eV due the band gap
being relatively small in this material. The hybrids (B,E) inherit the flaws of the BSE calculation,
having the second exciton peak being located at an energy too large. However, they are closer to
predicting the static refractive index. The Kernel Hybrid is closest, while the Simple Hybrid and
the two Вӯ H hybrids underestimate the static refractive index a bit. Considering the first excitonic
peak in (B,E), i.e. the peak at the lowest energy, the Kernel Hybrid does actually capture more of
the sharpness contrary to the BSE and the other hybrids. It does however overestimate the large
peak in the extinction coefficient (E), just above 3 eV. The fВӯsum (C) does well on this material,
only overestimating the static refractive index a bit. In (F) we see the EELS spectrum. Once again,
all hybrids follows the RPA calculation, and the large plasmonic peak at 20Вӯ30 eV is well captured.
Also a smaller peak around 10 eV seems to be present in the spectrum.
Let us now consider hexagonal boron nitride, hBN. This material too is a layered material conВӯ
sisting of hexagonally packed layers with van der Waals forces binding adjacent layers together.
Thus, like it was the case for MoS2, the material exhibits a large anisotropy. Another characteristic
property of hBN is its large band gap making it a good electric insulator [30]. The space group
is P63/mmc, and the lattice constants of hBN are found experimentally in [30]. In this paper, the
lattice parameters are given as a function of temperature. At a temperature of 98 K, they are found
to be a = b = 2.5058 Г… and c = 6.6090 Г…. In the calculation that we have done on hBN, we made
some initial DFT calculations in order to find the ground state eigenstates and Вӯenergies to be used
in the polarizabilities in the BSE and RPA calculations. You can read more about the theory of
DFT in section B and the implementation and actual calculations in this project in Appendix I. In
these DFT calculations, we had a FermiВӯDirac occupation width of kBT = 0.01, corresponding
to a temperature of 116 K. This applied to all the materials. Thus, the lattice parameters we have
chosen from the paper are at a temperature close to what we have worked with in this project, but
in hindsight it would have been nice to match the temperature in the DFT calculations on hBN to
one of the available ones from the paper. And also, there are lattice parameters available at 114
K, i.e. even closer to the calculation temperature of 116 K, and it would have made more sense to
choose these lattice parameters for the calculations. Another point to consider here is the fact that
we have derived the BSE and RPA at T=0, but actually use a small but finite temperature in the
DFT calculations. However, having some smearing in the DFT calculations in general a necessity
for the sake of convergence. During the iterations in DFT, we may imagine a band very close to
the Fermi level being in one iteration below the Fermi level and in the next iteration above, thus,
76
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids
==PAGE:83==
if we are at 0 K, going from having an occupation of 0 to an occupation of 1, thereby hindering
convergence. A small smearing is enough to avoid this problem. But the BSE and RPA assume 0
K so one may consider if this is a problematic disagreement. However, the occupation width used
will only introduce a very small occupation of the conduction bands when we work with semiconВӯ
ductors, so it seems reasonable to assume that the small temperature has a negligible effect on the
calculated ground state. For hBN we were able to include bands within Оҙ = 2.9 eV to each side
of the direct band gap corresponding to 5 valence and 2 conduction bands. The direct band gap
was fitted to the experimental data to be around 6.7 eV, and thus the cutoff frequency of hBN was
Пүc = 6.7eV + 2.9eV = 9.6eV. Details on the structure, calculation settings and convergence can
be found in appendix I, section I.0.4. For this material, due to a lack of time, we did not deterВӯ
mine the extinction coefficient, and we did not employ the fВӯsum method. Figure 7.5 shows our
predictions for the refractive index and EELS together with some experimental data.
A
B
C
Figure 7.5: Theoretical predictions for refractive index (A,B) and EELS (C) for hBN together with
experimental data. The experimental data for the refractive index was obtained from [31], and
experimental data for the EELS spectrum was obtained from [32].
In figure 7.5 (A) we see a large excitonic peak in the experimental data just above 6 eV captured by
the BSE but not by the RPA. The experimental data seems to suggest a static refractive at about 2.1Вӯ
2.3, which is a bit more than we have found with the fully converged RPA calculation. Considering
the hybrids (B), we see that the Simple Hybrid and the two Вӯ H hybrids predict the experimental data
very well. The Kernel Hybrid also finds a suitable static refractive index, but as we have also seen
for some of the other materials, particularly anatase TiO2, the Kernel Hybrid largely overestimates
the excitonic peak and also locates it at the wrong energy compared to the experimental data. The
EELS spectrum in (C) is plotted at lower energies than we have seen for the other materials up
until now. The first problem is that the RPA does not predict the right location for the largest peak
compared to the experimental data. According to the experimental data, the peak is around 7.5 eV,
whereas the RPA locates it at around 11 eV. Moreover, considering the EELS spectrum at lower
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids
77
==PAGE:84==
energies, the hybrids do not to follow the RPA calculation, as opposed to other EELS spectra that
we have considered. A possible explanation is that the peaks in the experimental data, both the
large peak at 7.5 eV but also the shoulder at approximately 6Вӯ7 eV, are influenced by excitons. The
shoulder, which is located just around the band gap of the material and thus most likely is a purely
excitonic peak, is reflected in all of the hybrids, all having a peak at around 7 eV. What is also
worth noticing is that the Simple Hybrid and the two Вӯ H hybrids, although not having the right
shape of the peak, do shift down the large peak in energy compared to RPA, moving it closer to
the experimental data. All calculations also have a rise in intensity at energies from 15 eV and up,
just as the experimental data show.
We finally consider HfS2. Like MoS2 it is a transition metal dichalcogenide with potential appliВӯ
cation in electronics and optoelectronics, see for example [33]. Here they mention that HfS2 is
predicted to have a carrier mobility 5 times higher than that of MoS2, and that it is predicted to be
an efficient photocatalyst for water splitting. The work on HfS2 differs from the other materials.
The BSE and RPA calculations have been made in close collaboration with one of my supervisors,
Mark Svendsen. The research section at which this project has been made, has a collaboration
with a neighboring experimental department, which has bought a HfS2 crystal that they intend to
measure the refractive index on. Thus, we would like to make predictions on the refractive index
for this material, and we do not beforehand have experimental data available for benchmarking.
Instead of using experimental lattice parameters, the lattice parameters of HfS2 were found by
with a DFT calculation with a soВӯcalled D3 relaxation, and were found to be a = b = 3.63 Г… and
c = 5.88 Г…. The space group is PВӯ3m1. We included bands within Оҙ = 2.6 eV to each side of the
band gap corresponding to 4 valence bands and 3 conduction bands. For the other materials, as
previously mentioned, we found the band gap by matching the location of the excitonic peaks in
the BSE calculation to the experimentally observed ones. Since we do not have experimental data
available, that is of course not possible. Instead, the band gap was found from a GW calculation
which provides much more reliable band gaps than DFT with the PBE functional. The GW band
gap was found to be 2.9 eV. Thus, for this system, Пүc = 2.9eV + 2.6eV = 5.5eV. A little more
information on the HfS2 calculations can be found in appendix I, section I.0.6. Figure 7.6 displays
the predicted refractive index and EELS spectrum.
The BSE calculation, figure 7.6 (A), displays two excitonic peaks below 3 eV, not captured by the
RPA calculations. Both the full RPA calculation and the BSE predict a static refractive index of
approximately 2.5 eV. It might be a coincidence that they are in agreement. The RPA calculation
has this value due to being converged with respect to the number of bands, whereas the BSE
calculation has a higher static refractive index than the finite RPA calculation due to the excitonic
peaks being close to zero energy and therefore influencing the static refractive index significantly
compared to higher energy transitions. We would like the hybrids to contain both effects, which
should result a higher static refractive index than the RPA and BSE calculations. The hybrids (B)
predict the excitonic peaks as well. As foreseen, the Simple and Kernel Hybrids predict a slightly
higher static refractive index, about 2.6Вӯ2.7, and the Вӯ H hybrids predict a static refractive index of
about 3.
The EELS spectrum (C) is very interesting here. The RPA, Simple and Kernel Hybrids are in agreeВӯ
ment, showing a large plasmonic peak around 30 eV. But the two Вӯ H hybrids lack this peak. The
two Вӯ H hybrids have their real parts of the polarizability determined through Hilbert transforms
of the imaginary parts. By considering plots of the polarizability found with the Вӯ H hybrids, we
could see that the transitions at lower frequencies cause a shift in the real part of the polarizability,
such that the real part does not follow the RPA calculated real part, even at very high frequencies.
For HfS2, this shift in the real part has the consequence that the poles in the RPA calculated diВӯ
electric function, which cause the peaks in the EELS spectrum, are simply not present in the Вӯ H
78
7.1. Hybrid model calculations for various solids
==PAGE:85==
A
B
C
Figure 7.6: Theoretical predictions for refractive index (A,B) and EELS (C) for HfS2.
hybrids. This reveals a serious flaw in the Вӯ H hybrids in regards to predicting the spectrum at large
energies. However, it also shines light on a more general issue. The Kernel and Simple Hybrids
do not obey the KramersВӯKronig due to the FermiВӯDirac factor that was imposed on them, and that
is unacceptable for any physically correct density response function or polarizability. The correcВӯ
tion is only present over the course of 1/2 eV, making the error relatively small, but due to the
KramersВӯKronig relation, the correction influences the real part of the spectrum nonВӯlocally. HowВӯ
ever, as seen, the price for imposing the KramersВӯKronig will in some cases be an EELS spectrum
that does not follow the RPA calculation. The Kernel and Simple Hybrids on the other hand will
follow the RPA at large energies per construction. So whether to choose hybrid functions which
obey the KramersВӯKronig relation or hybrid functions which we are sure always follow the RPA
at large energies is a dilemma.
7.2
Overestimated fВӯsums
An indicator of how well the hybrids perform is their fВӯsums. In theory, the wellВӯconverged RPA
calculation should obey the fВӯsum rule. This is due to the RPA being a soВӯcalled conserving
approximation [34], meaning that it is possible to show that the approximation of the selfВӯenergy
with pairВӯbubbles should result in a GreenвҖҷs function that conserves different physical quantities,
including the fВӯsum [35]. Thus, a hypothesis could be that the hybrid with the fВӯsum closest to
that of the RPA would predict the experimental data the best. Table 7.1 shows the fВӯsums of the
RPA and hybrids in units of eV/Г…3. The RPA should really obey the fВӯsum rule before we do the
shift in energies, вҲҶE, that make the polarizabilities match the material band gap. Therefore we
have calculated the RPA fВӯsum before and after this energy shift (band gap corrected).

before (first column) and after (second column) we have corrected for the underestimation of the

fВӯsums for each of the materials in consideration. Note that the Simple Hybrid and the Simple
Hybrid Вӯ H have the same fВӯsum since they only differ in the real part part of the polarizability.
Considering table 7.1 we are not surprised to see that the band gap corrected RPA always has a
larger fВӯsum than the original RPA calculated polarizability, and the hybrids are influenced by this,
having fВӯsums closer to the band gap corrected RPA. We tried to impose the fВӯsum on the band gap
corrected finite and full RPA calculations by multiplying the spectra by ПүвҲ’вҲҶE
Пү
which does indeed
yield the correct fВӯsums, but in the end yields hybrids that do not predict the experimental data very
well. While the RPA is in principle a conserving approximation before the band gap correction,
it is an open question how the correction affects this property. Shifting the energies introduces
a mismatch between the energies and wavefunctions. Had we performed the DFT calculation
with a selfВӯconsistent GW selfВӯenergy instead of PBE, we would have obtained a more physically
correct band structure and corresponding wavefunctions. This might make the band gap correction
unnecessary, and we would avoid the mismatch.
If we accept the fВӯsum of the band gap corrected RPA and compare it to the fВӯsums of the hybrids,
we see that especially the Kernel Hybrid tends to overestimate the fВӯsum, whereas the Simple
Hybrid and the two Вӯ H hybrids only overestimate it a bit. However, rutile TiO2, eВӯaxis, and MoS2
were described well by the Kernel Hybrid. Besides, we used the fВӯsum method on all the TiO2
variants and MoS2, finding that in general the prediction of the fВӯsum method was not as good
as the Simple Hybrid and the Вӯ H hybrids. The fВӯsum rule specifically imposes the fВӯsum, and
thus we have not included it in the tableНҫ this fВӯsum is by construction almost exactly equal to the
fВӯsum of the original RPA calculated polarizability. Thus, we do not believe that aiming for the
right fВӯsum necessarily yields the best results.
One can also question whether the RPA calculation completely hits the right fВӯsum. For one thing,
we only converged the RPA calculation up to 60 eV, and in theory we should converge to infinite
energies. The slightly higher fВӯsums of the Simple Hybrid and the Kernel Hybrid, which do better
than the RPA and BSE calculations in predicting the spectra we have considered, may be closer to
the true fВӯsum, since one could hypothesize that the full RPA would have reached this fВӯsum had
we converged to even higher frequencies. Another point to make is that we are using KohnВӯSham
calculated eigenstates and Вӯenergies, which may be problematic because the RPA derivation takes
outset in the Hartree picture. The use of KohnВӯSham states is an improvement over the Hartree
states since in this way we are able to incorporate exchange and correlation effects via the PBE
exchangeВӯcorrelation potential that the Hartree states do not contain. But it has the consequence
that when calculating the RPA polarizability, we have a mix of approximations entering the exВӯ
pressions, using KohnВӯSham eigenvalues and Вӯenergies but Hartree electronВӯelectron interactions.
As a consequence, the fВӯsum rule might not be exactly conserved.
80
7.2. Overestimated fВӯsums
==PAGE:87==
It remains undetermined to what extent the overestimated fВӯsums of the hybrids are a problem in
regards to their ability to predict the correct physical spectra, and how corrections should be imВӯ
posed, if that is the case. An easy way to improve the predictions would be using selfВӯconsistent
GW in the DFT calculations instead of PBE. When we have not done so it is due to GW calculaВӯ
tions being more computationally demanding.
7.3
Hybrid function selection across materials
For most materials, the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H and Transition Вӯ H are in agreement at both higher
and lower frequencies. There are mainly two differences between these two hybrids. The first
difference is the way they transition from a frequency range where we have a BSE calculation,
to a frequency range where we only have the fully converged RPA. The Transition Вӯ H hybrid
transitions in an interpolating way over the course of 2 eV, while the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H transitions
through a FermiВӯDirac factor working over 1/2 eV, as described in chapter 5. In principle the way
of transitioning can influence the entire spectrum due to the Hilbert transform. Further, recall
that the BSE polarizability has a contribution that is equal to the finite RPA calculation. Another
difference between them is that in the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H, this RPA contribution is replaced by
the fully converged RPA calculation, where in the Transition Вӯ H it is not. We do also see very
small differences between them in the spectra, see for example the refractive index for bulk MoS2,
figure 7.4 (B), and rutile TiO2, eВӯaxis, figure 7.2 (B). But the differences seem negligible. The
two hybrids seem to do rather well in predicting the experimental data. Especially rutile TiO2, oВӯ
axis, anatase TiO2 and hBN are very well predicted by these two hybrids, both at higher and lower
frequencies. However, the main flaw in these hybrids is the risk that the Hilbert transform may
lift the real part of the Вӯ H polarizabilities in such a way that we do not obtain the desired EELS
spectrum, like we saw for HfS2, figure 7.6 (C).
In general the Simple Hybrid is very similar to the Вӯ H predictions. However, not having the
Hilbert transform means that the refractive indices are slightly smaller than the two Вӯ H hybrids for
all materials. The two Вӯ H hybrids are in general slightly closer to the experimental data for the
refractive indices, see rutile TiO2, eВӯaxis, figure 7.2 (B), MoS2, figure 7.4 (B), and hBN, figure 7.5
(B), and in the remaining two cases, rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis, figure 7.1 and anatase TiO2, figure 7.3, the
lowest frequency experimental data is sandwiched between the Simple Hybrid and the Вӯ H hybrids.
But the differences between these three hybrids are quite small and the shapes of the graphs are
almost identical. The fact the Simple Hybrid, as opposed to the Вӯ H hybrids, does not obey the
KramersВӯKronig relation is a serious flaw of this method even though the error is small. But on
the other hand, the higher frequency EELS spectra are not in risk of failing. Choosing between
Simple Hybrid and the Вӯ H hybrids then poses a dilemma. The right choice probably depends on
the intended use. If we are only interested in the low frequency spectrum, like the refractive index,
then the Вӯ H hybrids yield the most accurate results. If, on the other hand, you would like to predict
the polarizability at all frequencies, then the Simple Hybrid is a safer choice.
The fВӯsum method did well in predicting the refractive index of MoS2, but otherwise it has a
tendency to overestimate the static refractive index. From the way it is constructed it can not be
used to predict higher frequency spectra.
The Kernel Hybrid has a tendency to sometimes overestimate the height of the peaks, and also the
fВӯsum of this hybrid lies further away from that of the fully converged RPA calculation than the
three other hybrids. However, it seems to do better in predicting the refractive index for MoS2
and rutile TiO2, eВӯaxis, than the Simple Hybrid and the two Вӯ H hybrids, see figure 7.2 and 7.4
respectively. But that means that in 3/5 cases the Kernel Hybrid overestimated the height of the
exciton peaks, that is for rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis, anatase TiO2 and hBN. Here, the Simple Hybrid and
the two ВӯH functions did much better in predicting the experimental refractive index, including
the exciton peak heights. So letвҖҷs consider what happens. Recall the expression for the Kernel
7.3. Hybrid function selection across materials
81
==PAGE:88==
Hybrid, eq. (5.16). We will be ignoring the FermiВӯDirac factor, which only works over a short
energy interval, and also lies beyond the energy interval, that we consider here anyway. What we
then see is that the denominator of the Kernel Hybrid has a pole when the real part of the full RPA
calculation times the kernel is 1, and if at the same time the imaginary part is 0. In figure 7.7 we
see a plot of the real and imaginary part of the full RPA calculated polarizability times the kernel,
which is the inverse finite RPA calculated polarizability minus the BSE calculated polarizability,
for each of the materials considered in this project.

Figure 7.7: Real of imaginary part of the RPA polarizability times the kernel from the denominator
of the Kernel Hybrid for each of the materials considered in this project. A horizontal dashed line
marks 1 on the yВӯaxis since the real part of the denominator of the Kernel Hybrid has a pole here.
When this line is intersected by the real part, we have also made a vertical dashed line to indicate
what the value of the imaginary part is at this energy.
What figure 7.7 reveals is that in the three cases, where the Kernel Hybrid behaves badly, the real
part intersects 1. The imaginary part of rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis, is about 0.3, for anatase TiO2 it is about
0.1, and for hBN less than 0.1. This is consistent with the fact that Kernel Hybrid performs worse
on anatase TiO2 and hBN, and is better in the case of rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis. In the case of rutile TiO2,
eВӯaxis, and MoS22, the real part does not intersect 1 at all, and this is also where the Kernel Hybrid
is the hybrid making the prediction closest to experiments. This indicates a possible criterion for
when to apply the Kernel Hybrid and when to apply the Simple Hybrid. If the real part of the
RPA calculated polarizability times the kernel intersects 1 at a point where the imaginary part is
below some threshold, we should apply the Simple Hybrid, and if not, we should apply the Kernel
Hybrid. To determine if this criterion holds and what the threshold should be, would require much
more benchmarking than done in this project.
7.4
Kernel Hybrid function with local field effects
We have investigated the effect of including local field effects in the Kernel Hybrid more directly.
That means using eq. (5.15), ПҮKH
GGвҖІ, for the Kernel Hybrid instead of eq. (5.16), ПҮKH, which we
have been using up until now, the difference being that the polarizabilities entering the expression
are now matrices with the entries corresponding to reciprocal lattice vectors G, GвҖІ. It is not that
local field effects were not included before, using eq. (5.16) for the Kernel Hybrid. Recall back
from eq. (3.42) that the polarizability is linked to the macroscopic dielectric function, which is
defined as one over the G = GвҖІ = 0 component of the inverse dielectric function. The inversion
introduces local field effects, and since our expression with the polarizability is equivalent to this,
82
7.4. Kernel Hybrid function with local field effects
==PAGE:89==
the polarizabilities entering the Kernel Hybrid (5.16) did hold information about the local fields.
But in this section we introduce the results from including local field effects fully into the Kernel
Hybrid.
In making this investigation, we make use of the results from chapter 6. For the BSE, we need
to include the TDA since the calculations are otherwise too computationally heavy. But since the
TDA does not work well if we include the longВӯrange Coulomb interactions, we need to work with
the polarizabilities instead of the density response functions. This may seem like a trivial note,
having worked with the polarizabilities so far anyway. But due to the way the GPAW codes are
created, this was important to take into account during the implementation.
In figure 7.8 is shown the refractive index and EELS spectrum for rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis, calculated
with Kernel Hybrids with and without direct local field effects together with experimental data. In
general, we calculated the polarizability for this material with a plane wave cutoff of 150 eV, but in
this investigation it is reduced to 50 eV corresponding to matrices that contain 47 Г— 47 reciprocal
lattice vectors. This was done in order to obtain smaller polarizability matrices, since we needed
to perform calculations with them to find the Kernel Hybrid. We also tried a cutoff of 100 eV, but
the result was worse than what we see in figure 7.8.
B
A
Figure 7.8: Kernel Hybrid function with local field effects constructed from polarizability matrices

From figure 7.8 we see that the method does not seem too promising. Comparing to the Kernel
Hybrid without direct local field effects, the static refractive index is perhaps closer to the experiВӯ
mental one, and the peak is less overestimated, but some very strange and large peaks has appeared
around 5 eV. Considering the EELS spectrum, we see that the overall trends are followed, but we
have a very sharp divergent peak around 7 eV. From considering the inverted expression in the
Kernel Hybrid matrices, eq. (5.15), i.e. the 1 вҲ’ПҮRPA Г— kernel, at different frequencies, we know
that they are very numerically unstable, i.e. they have determinants very close to zero, and so the
inversion does not go well. The problem increases when more matrix entries are included in the
polarizabilities entering the Kernel Hybrid. Thus, we are actually able to sort of avoid the problem
by only considering a subset of the matrix. The strange peaks around 5 eV in the refractive index
and 7 eV in the EELS spectrum first appear when the matrices include 26 Г— 26 reciprocal lattice
vectors. Thus, in figure 7.9 we have included only the first 25 Г— 25 entries.
The number of 25Г—25 entries is found from eyesight. Thus, this approach of reducing the matrices
is rather questionable. Considering figure 7.9, the beginning of a peak around 5 eV has begun to
appear, even though it seems small enough at this point to not really pose a problem. Besides, it is
