8
Conclusion and Outlook
8.1
Conclusion
This project described how the electric susceptibility, also called the density response function, can
be used to determine important optical features of materials. More specifically, we saw that from
the density response function, we can determine absorption spectra, electron energy loss spectra,
refractive indices and extinction coefficients, which are all important information when one wishes
to characterize a solid. We explained that some important effects that can sometimes be observed
in these spectra are plasmons and excitons at higher and lower frequencies respectively, and modВӯ
els for the density response function should then ideally capture these effects. To model the density
response function, we began by defining it through linear response theory. We derived an expresВӯ
sion for the density response function using timeВӯdependent density functional theory (TDDFT)
in reciprocal space, and saw that even though this expression was exact, we did not know the
exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel entering the equation and needed to find approximations to that. We
introduced the irreducible density response function, also called the polarizability. The polarizВӯ
ability is characterized by having the longВӯrange component of the Coulomb interaction put to
zero during its calculation. Otherwise, all equations which apply to the density response function,
also apply to the polarizability. We then introduced manyВӯbody perturbation theory, and derived
the Random Phase Approximation (RPA). We recognized the RPA expression to be exactly the
TDDFT density response function without the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel. The RPA, containing
the longВӯrange Coulomb interaction, is able to capture plasmonic effects in the solids. But since
the RPA is a mean field approximation to the interactions in the solid, it is incapable of describing
direct twoВӯparticle interactions, and thus it does not capture excitonic effects. We then derived the
BetheВӯSalpeter equation (BSE) which does capture these important effects, but unfortunately it is
a computationally heavy method. We introduced the TammВӯDancoff approximation which is emВӯ
ployed in order reduce the computational cost. But even then, we are only actually able to include
a few bands around the Fermi level in the BSE calculations on real materials.
Exploiting the fact that excitons are typically most important for low frequency transitions, while
plasmons dominate the highВӯfrequency spectrum, we proposed four different hybrid functions suitВӯ
able for the calculation of the density response function over a wide range of frequencies. These
four hybrid functions, which we called the Kernel Hybrid, the Simple Hybrid, the Simple Hybrid
Вӯ H and Transition Вӯ H, were composed of a finite BSE calculation together with a fully converged
RPA calculation, and sometimes also a finite RPA calculation having the same bands included as
the BSE calculation. These hybrid functions also address the issue that the real and imaginary parts
of the density response function are connected via the KramersВӯKronig relation, making the real
part a nonВӯlocal function that depends on the entirety of the imaginary part, which means that it is
often not converged when only few bands are included in the calculation.
8.1.1
The TammВӯDancoff approximation
An important insight from this project is that one needs to be cautious when using the TammВӯ
Dancoff approximation in the BSE. The scaling problems of the BSE means that the approximaВӯ
tion can be a necessity. But for some systems it tends to change the results drastically. A way
to improve the results is to include the negative transitions when calculating the polarizabilities
exploiting that the G = GвҖІ = 0 component is antiВӯsymmetric in the frequency domain, either
by directly including these transitions or by performing a Hilbert transform on the imaginary part
of the calculated polarizability under this constraint. For systems where the coupling part of the
matrix is small, this yields quite accurate results, as we saw in the case of silicon. Including the
negative frequencies is also an improvement for systems where the coupling part is stronger. We
Chapter 8. Conclusion and Outlook
85
==PAGE:92==
also saw that the TammВӯDancoff approximation works better when the longВӯrange component of
the Coulomb interaction is not included in the calculations. Thus, we should determine the polarВӯ
izability with the BSE code instead of the density response function.
8.1.2
Hybrid models for predicting experimental data
RPA, finite RPA and BSE calculated polarizabilities were used to create the four hybrids, and
sometimes also the fВӯsum method. All these calculated hybrid polarizabilities were then used to
determine the refractive indices, EELS spectra, and sometimes also the extinction coefficients for
the materials rutile TiO2 with the incoming electric field polarized along the ordinary and extraorВӯ
dinary axes respectively, anatase TiO2, bulk MoS22, hBN and HfS2. Except for HfS2, we were
in most cases able to benchmark the predicted spectra against experimental data. All hybrids in
general did well in predicting the experimental data, in particular much better than the RPA and
BSE calculations. As expected from the theory, the BSE calculations tend to underestimate the
static refractive indices, and the fully converged RPA calculations did not capture the excitonic
peaks. Besides, sometimes the RPA will also underestimate the static refractive index, when this
is influenced by excitons present at low frequencies. In all cases, the hybrids predicted the exciton
peaks and with few exceptions, they also predicted the static refractive indices quite well. Except
in the case of HfS2, all hybrids followed the full RPA calculated EELS spectrum as desired, thus
accurately describing the plasmons present in the experimental EELS spectra. When excitonic
peaks were present in the experimental EELS spectra, these were not captured by the RPA, but all
hybrids predicted these peaks.
We saw that most often the Simple Hybrid, the Simple Hybrid Вӯ H and the Transition Вӯ H yield
very similar predictions, especially the two latter hybrids. However, as seen for HfS2, the Hilbert
transform performed in the two Вӯ H hybrids for finding their real parts means that the spectrum is
affected everywhere by the imaginary part at smaller frequencies. Thus, the EELS spectra from
the two ВӯH hybrids might not follow the RPA calculated EELS spectra as they should. The Simple
Hybrid and Kernel Hybrid do not obey the KramersВӯKronig relation due to the FermiВӯDirac factor
imposed in these models. But the risk of not obtaining the correct EELS spectrum means that we
are leaning towards using the Simple and Kernel Hybrids for predicting full spectra. However, if
the spectrum is only needed at lower frequencies in some specific use case, e.g. for finding the
refractive index, the two Вӯ H hybrids work very well. Comparing the Simple Hybrid and the Kernel
Hybrid, we would say that overall the Simple Hybrid did best in predicting the low energy spectra
for rutile TiO2, oВӯaxis, anatase TiO2 and hBN, while the Kernel Hybrid did better in predicting
the spectra for rutile TiO2, eВӯaxis, and MoS2. There seemingly appears to be a tendency for the
Kernel Hybrid to fail when part of the spectrum is close to one of its poles. In these cases however,
the Simple Hybrid did very well. And when there were no poles in the denominator of the Kernel
Hybrid, it did better than the Simple Hybrid. Thus, a possible idea is to test if such a pole is present
in the Kernel Hybrid, perhaps simultaneously considering the value of the imaginary part at the
frequency point of the pole. If the pole is present, and if the imaginary part of the denominator is
below some threshold value, one should use the Simple Hybrid, and otherwise, one should use the
Kernel Hybrid.
8.2
Outlook
Many potentially interesting conclusions can be drawn from this project, but further investigations
are necessary in order to confirm that they hold in general. The validity of the idea of selecting a
hybrid function based on whether a pole is present in the real part of the denominator of the Kernel
Hybrid, as explained in section 7.3, lacks statistical evidence. Our results point in that direction,
but we would need to test it on many more materials to be sure. In general, we need to test the
hybrids on many more materials where we have experimental data available.
Further, the hybrids all had the problem of overestimating the fВӯsums, mostly due to them being
86
8.2. Outlook
==PAGE:93==
based on RPA and BSE calculations that had been band gap corrected, since the shift in energies
introduced a mismatch between the energies and wavefunctions entering the expressions for the
density response functions or polarizabilities. Therefore we would like the band gap corrected
polarizabilities, i.e. the full RPA but also the finite BSE and RPA calculated polarizabilities, to
have the same fВӯsum after the correction. One way to approach this problem could be to base the
BSE and RPA calculations on selfВӯconsistent GW calculations instead of using a PBE exchangeВӯ
correlation functional in the DFT calculations as we did in this project. Alternatively, we could
look for another way of correcting for the change in fВӯsums upon the band gap correction.
It is a problem that the Kernel and Simple Hybrids do not obey the KramersВӯKronig relation due to
the FermiВӯDirac factor. We imposed the FermiВӯDirac factor in these two hybrids to prevent them
from dragging the hybrid polarizability down to zero instead of having it approach the full RPA
calculation, as explained in chapter 5. It is desirable to develop an alternative way of achieving the
same purpose that does not have the unintentional consequence that the hybrid no longer obeys the
KramersВӯKronig relation.
In general, the BSE calculations did well in predicting the shape of the refractive indices of the
materials considered in this project, although as also expected, the values for the refractive indices
were underestimated. However, in particular for bulk MoS2, one of the peaks present in the exВӯ
perimental data was not present in the BSE calculation, figure 7.4. The hybrids will never be able
to predict an excitonic peak that was not present in the BSE calculation, and so, to obtain better
results, one could consider ways to improve the BSE calculation. One way could be to do the
calculations without the TammВӯDancoff approximation. In this project, using the approximation
was necessary to perform the calculations due to the computational cost. If the cost of calculations
could be reduced, for instance by improving the GPAW implementation of the BSE, it is possible
that we would be able to perform calculations without the TammВӯDancoff approximation, at least
for some materials. Further, in the derivation of the BSE in the GW approximation, we assumed
that the screened interaction W was instantaneous, such that the BSE matrix was not a function of
frequency. One could imagine implementing a frequency dependence to improve the BSE calculaВӯ
tions. This could perhaps be based on the recently proposed method by [36], where the frequency
dependence of W is described in an efficient multipole approximation.
Finally, it would be interesting to understand exactly why it was problematic to include local field
effects in the Kernel Hybrid. There was a tendency for the local field effects to correct for the flaws
of the Kernel Hybrid Вӯ the location and height of the peak and the static refractive matched the
experimental data better than the Kernel Hybrid without local field effects Вӯ but rapid oscillations
or divergent peaks would start to appear when more matrix entries were included in the calculation.
We do not fully understand why these peaks appear or why creating the Kernel Hybrid using only
subsets of the RPA and BSE polarizability matrices would reduce the problem. Understanding why
this happens could potentially enable us to correct the model to generate better predictions.
8.2. Outlook
87

B
Density Functional Theory (DFT)
Consider the full Hamiltonian of interacting electrons and nuclei [37],
ЛҶH = ЛҶTe + ЛҶTn + ЛҶuee + ЛҶVion + ЛҶvnn + ЛҶvext
(B.1)
where ЛҶTe and ЛҶTn are the kinetic energies of the electrons and nuclei respectively, and ЛҶuee, ЛҶVion
and ЛҶvnn are the interacting potentials between the electrons, the nuclei and electrons and the nuclei
respectively, and ЛҶvext is an external potential. The problem can be further simplified using the BornВӯ
Oppenheimer approximation: the nuclei are about 105 times heavier than the electrons, and thus
the lattice ions move slowly around their equilibrium positions compared to the lighter electrons.
We therefore consider them to be fixed to their positions, meaning that ЛҶTn = 0, and the repulsive
potential ЛҶvnn becomes a constant with no relevance for the Hamiltonian. We therefore also put
ЛҶvnn = 0. ЛҶVion now accounts for the fact that the fixed nuclei generate a static potential in which
the electrons are moving. We are left with
ЛҶH = ЛҶTe + ЛҶuee + ЛҶVion + ЛҶVext.
(B.2)
With this Hamiltonian, we could in principle solve the SchrГ¶dinger equation to obtain eigenvalues
and Вӯenergies, but being too computationally expensive [37], DFT makes a reformulation of the
problem. Here, the ground state electron density, n0(r), becomes the central variable instead of the
manyВӯbody wave function. This reduces the problem considerably since the density is a function
of only three spatial variables, whereas the full wave function for N electrons will be a function
of 3N variables. Making this reformulation is justified by the HohenbergВӯKohn theorems. The
ground state energy is then a functional of the ground state density,
E0[n0] = Te[n0] + uee[n0] + Vion[n0] + Vext[n0],
(B.3)
where e.g.
Te[n0] = вҹЁПҲ0| ЛҶTe|ПҲ0вҹ©.
(B.4)
The potential from the ions can be written in terms of the ground state density as
Vion[n0] =
Z
vion(r)n0(r)dr,
(B.5)
where
vion(r) = вҲ’e2
P
X
i=1
Zi
|r вҲ’Ri|.
(B.6)
P is the number of nuclei, Z is the atomic number and Ri is the position of nucleus number i. As
we have seen before, the external potential has this form as well,
Vext[n0] =
Z
vext(r)n0(r)dr.
(B.7)
By minimizing the total energy with respect to the density we can find the ground state density n0,
ОҙE[n]
Оҙn(r) = 0.
(B.8)
Appendix B. Density Functional Theory (DFT)
91
==PAGE:98==
From this density we would then be able to determine all other quantities of interest. However, we
only know an exact expression for the external potential. The kinetic energy, Te and the electronВӯ
electron interaction potential, uee are unknowns and we need to approximate them. In the KohnВӯ
Sham formulation, Te is treated as the kinetic energy Ts of a corresponding nonВӯinteracting system
while uee is approximated by the Hartree energy,
EH =
Z Z n(rвҖІ)n(r)
|r вҲ’rвҖІ| drdrвҖІ,
(B.9)
and an exchangeВӯcorrelation term Exc which accounts for manyВӯbody effects of the system, such
that the total energy is now
E[n] = Ts[n] + 1
2
Z Z n(rвҖІ)n(r)
|r вҲ’rвҖІ| drdrвҖІ +
Z
(vion(r) + vext(r)) n(r)dr + Exc[n].
(B.10)
By taking the functional derivative of this we obtain the KohnВӯSham equation. We define
vR[n](r) = vH[n](r) + vion(r) + ОҙExc[n]
Оҙn(r) + vext(r)
(B.11)
where we defined the Hartree potential as,
vH[n](r) =
Z
n(rвҖІ)
|r вҲ’rвҖІ|drвҖІ.
(B.12)
In this way we have obtained an expression describing a fictitious system of nonВӯinteracting elecВӯ
trons generating the same density of a system of interacting electrons through this effective potenВӯ
tial. The SchrГ¶dinger equation for the fictitious system of electrons then becomes,

вҲ’вҲҮ2
2m + vR(r)

П•i(r) = ОөiП•i(r).
(B.13)
From the solution to this equation we can find the ground state density as
n(r) =
N
X
i=1
fi|П•i(r)|2,
(B.14)
where fi is the occupation number. This is a selfВӯconsistency problem which can be solved iterВӯ
atively for the density. We provide an initial guess for the singleВӯparticle orbitals, solve for the
electron density, and use this density to create a new set of orbitals, until the new set of orbitals
are consistent with the starting set of orbitals. However, the exchangeВӯcorrelation potential is unВӯ
known. There have been developed some different functionals to approximate it, for example LDA
and PBE. Accounting for these goes beyond the scope of this report.
From the procedure described here we can obtain the eigenstates and Вӯenergies needed to determine
the nonВӯinteracting density response function, eqs. (3.24, 3.38), in terms of the nonВӯinteracting
KohnВӯSham system.

As an example of WickвҖҷs theorem, consider the expectation value of a product of four operators:
вҹЁЛҶcвҖ 
1ЛҶc2ЛҶcвҖ 
3ЛҶc4вҹ©.

Using WickвҖҷs theorem, this can be expressed as a sum of all possible contractions:
вҹЁЛҶcвҖ 
1ЛҶc2ЛҶcвҖ 
3ЛҶc4вҹ©= вҹЁЛҶcвҖ

3вҹ©,
(E.19)
where the first term corresponds to the case where the first two operators are contracted, the second
term corresponds to the case where the first and third operators are contracted, and so on. Note
that to obtain the middle term we interchanged ЛҶc2 and ЛҶcвҖ 
3 which introduced a sign change, but in
this example, the term will not contribute since it contains the expectation value of two creation
(annihilation) operators which is zero. In the last term we interchange ЛҶcвҖ 
3 and ЛҶc4 which introduces
a sign change, but then we interchange ЛҶc2 and ЛҶc4 introducing once more a sign change, and ends
up with a positive sign.

F
Hedin Equations
In this appendix we derive the Hedin equations with outset in [10]. Initially, we need to derive an
expression for the exchangeВӯcorrelation selfВӯenergy. For this, we first need to once more consider
eq. (4.59), where we derived the EOM for G and G2. If we neglect all interactions, G simplifies
to the nonВӯinteracting G0 and G2 disappears from the EOM, such that the equation becomes,
(iвҲӮt1 вҲ’Оө(1))G0(1, 2) = Оҙ(1, 2) вҮ’GвҲ’1
0 (1, 2) = (iвҲӮt1 вҲ’Оө(1))Оҙ(1, 2).
(F.1)

GвҲ’1
0 (1, ВҜ3)G(ВҜ3, 2) = (iвҲӮt1 вҲ’Оө(1))Оҙ(1, ВҜ3)G(ВҜ3, 2) = (iвҲӮt1 вҲ’Оө(1))G(1, 2).
(F.2)
Now multiplying both sides by G0(4, 1) and integrating over 1 we have

104
==PAGE:111==
The Hedin equations can now be solved iteratively for G and the selfВӯenergy ОЈ: the exchangeВӯ
correlation part of the selfВӯenergy used to calculate G in the fifth of equations (F.21) is the selfВӯ
energy calculated using the same G in the second of the equations (F.21). The selfВӯenergy and
GreenвҖҷs function obtained through this process can then be used to solve the BSE for the twoВӯ
particle correlation function 4ПҮ. However, in practical applications it is not possible to find a conВӯ
vergent behavior in this set of equations and one need to employ the soВӯcalled GWВӯapproximation.

The Bare and Screened Interaction in
Reciprocal Space and BandВӯMomentum
Basis
This appendix takes outset in a loose mix between [10] and [18], but none of them derive these
expressions very explicitly, so the sources are only used for validation and to align definitions and

I
Calculation Settings and Convergence
This section accounts for the structural setup of the materials that we have made calculations on
in this project, and we show how many bands and kВӯpoints are required to obtain converged data
through convergence tests. We need in total three different calculations in order to construct the
hybrid functions as explained in chapter 5: a BSEF calculation, an RPAF and an RPA calculation.
The two finite calculations are not converged with respect to the number of bands. As explained
in section 4.4, the BSE calculation is too computationally costly to include a converged number
of bands. We will however, as shown in figure 5.1, aim at including bands at around 2 eV above
and below the band gap in order to catch the excitonic effects. Of course, we include more if
we can, but accept to include less if the number of kВӯpoints otherwise will be unacceptably low
convergence wise.
There are lots of parameters in the calculations that one could converge on. We have chosen to only
consider the number of bands and kВӯpoints, and all other parameters are put to standard settings.
So letвҖҷs start by considering these.
For all materials, two calculators were made in total. The initial calculator did the DFT ground
state calculation to find the ground state density. In this calculator we used a plane wave cutoff of
800 eV, the exchangeВӯcorrelation kernel was PBE, and we allowed for a FermiВӯDirac occupation
width of 0.01, corresponding to 116 K, in order to have a finite, but low, temperature. The О“Вӯpoint
was included in the list of kВӯpoints. We converged on the number of kВӯpoints in this calculator with
respect to the ground state energy.
The next calculator held the density constant and generated the kВӯpoints used in the actual polarВӯ
izability calculations. We made this second calculator instead of using the one described above
because the full RPA calculation often required more kВӯpoints to be properly converged than what
was needed for the ground state density to be converged, while the finite BSE calculation could
only be performed if we included fewer kВӯpoints than what was used in the first calculator. Thus,
this second calculator was made in two versions.
One of them was used in the full RPA calculation, and we made convergence tests for each material
to find a suitable number of kВӯpoints and bands to be included in these calculations by considering
the resulting polarizability. When the polarizability plots stopped changing when more kВӯpoints
or bands were added, we had reached convergence. In this calculation, we chose a broadening of
О· = 0.05 eV.
The other version had much fewer kВӯpoints and was used for the finite BSE calculation. To find
out how many kВӯpoints should be included in this calculator, we did a kВӯpoint convergence test
on the finite RPA calculation. When the convergence test was done on the finite RPA calculation
instead of the BSE calculation, it is simply because this is much computationally cheaper. And it
seems reasonable to assume that when the finite RPA calculation, having exactly as many bands
as the finite BSE calculation, was converged, then the BSE calculation could be assumed to be
converged too. However, it was not always possible to use a perfectly converged number of kВӯ
points in the BSE calculations, and in this case this convergence test was merely a sanity check:
we may have to accept that the calculation is not perfectly kВӯpoint converged, but the overall trends
in the plots should not change anymore when we were around the number of kВӯpoints that we were
able to include in the BSE calculation. If the overall trends in the plots were still changing at the
maximum possible number of kВӯpoints, we would have to accept including fewer bands. Including
fewer bands means that we have room for more kВӯpoints, and also the number of kВӯpoints converged
110
Appendix I. Calculation Settings and Convergence
==PAGE:117==
faster. We used a broadening of О· = 0.1 eV in BSE calculations, which we justify in convergence
section on the TiO2, rutile, section I.0.1.
Also the finite RPA calculation should to be converged with respect to the number of kВӯpoints, as
also described in the main text, section 5.1, and since we could include many kВӯpoints in this finite
RPA calculation, we also used a smaller broadening of 0.05 eV instead of the 0.1 eV that was used
in the BSE calculations. Thus, the convergence tests done on the finite RPA calculation to find
the number of kВӯpoints needed for the BSE calculation, could not be used directly. However, we
did not perform the convergence test on the finite RPA calculation directly, since we could just use
the result from the convergence test of the full RPA calculation. If the full RPA calculation was
converged at some specific kВӯpoint density, then the smaller RPA calculation would of course also
be converged at this point, having the same broadening but fewer bands. So we just reused this
result.
When the calculators were made, we did the polarizability calculations. Also here are some difВӯ
ferent parameters that one could consider. We had to select a plane wave cutoff Ecut. The plane
wave cutoff is a variable that we will often be specifying in the result section. This parameter is
the local field effect cutoff in the dielectric function.
Д§2|q + G|2
2m
вүӨEcut
(I.1)
The plane wave cutoff restricts the number of plane waves used in the calculation, such that all plane
waves obey eq. I.1. We chose to not converge on this parameter: the matrices have dimensions
corresponding to the number of GGвҖІ vectors, and therefore we were often limited in how large a
plane wave cutoff we could have. The code would crash due to CPU memory problems if the plane
wave cutoff was too large when the code tried to temporarily store the matrix. And so, the plane
wave cutoff was just put to 100 eV for most materials, unless otherwise stated.
The parameter О· was chosen to be small for the two RPA calculations, we chose it to be 50 meV,
since we can include as many kВӯpoints as we desire as described above, and therefore may aim
for large precision. For the BSE calculation, we did a small study on one of the materials, TiO2
in rutile phase, to find a suitable О·, and as discussed in the next subsection, we decided to have a
broadening of 100 meV based on this small study. Due to lack of time the investigation was not
repeated on all materials, but that is something that could have been nice to have. Instead, we kept
using the 100 meV for О·.
We will specify the kВӯpoint density dk instead of directly the number of kВӯpoints. The number of
kВӯpoints can be found using that the density is related to the number of kВӯpoints in each lattice
direction as
dki = N ai
2ПҖ,
(I.2)
where ai are the lattice vectors. We have used the вҖҷeven=TrueвҖҷ function in GPAW, meaning that
the number of kВӯpoints are rounded up to the nearest even number. We have done a BSE and RPA
calculation using the GPAW response codes вҖҷbse.pyвҖҷ and вҖҷdf.pyвҖҷ respectively. Documentation and
codes can be found in [38] and [39]. The kВӯpoint densities have the dimension of length, and we
measure them in units of kВӯpoints per Г…вҲ’1. In the following when giving numerical values for
kВӯpoint densities, we will not always state the unit, but it is always implicitly there.
In the following, many yВӯaxes will have the name ПҮ. In the main text we used this symbol for the
density response function, whereas we used ВҜПҮ for the polarizability. However, in the following
sections we will use ПҮ for the polarizability.
111
==PAGE:118==
I.0.1
Rutile TiO2
According to [23] the experimental lattice parameters of rutile TiO2 are a = 4.584 Г… and c =
2.953 Г…. The spacegroup is number 136 and we used atomic position basis (0, 0, 0), (0.3, 0.3, 0.0)
obtained from [24]. The angles between the lattice vectors are 90, 90 and 90 degrees. The BSE
and both RPA calculations were made with a plane wave cutoff at 150 eV.
As seen in figure 5.1 and also mentioned in the section 7, we include bands within Оҙ = 2 eV,
yielding 6 valence bands and 6 conduction bands in the finite calculations. The direct band gap
was found by fitting the outputted BSE polarizability to the experimental data, and was found to
be about 3.4 eV, yielding a cutoff frequency of Пүc = 3.4eV + 2eV = 5.4eV.
As described in the previous subsection, we made a convergence test on the ground state energy
on the first calculator. The result for rutile TiO2 is shown in figure I.1, left. We see that at around
a kВӯpoint density of 3Вӯ4, the ground state density is converged, so in the calculations of the poВӯ
larizabilities we made sure to be above this limit. Note that the yВӯscale is logarithmic. We then
converged on the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator by considering the resulting plots of
ПҮRPA
F
at a broadening of 50 meV. The results are shown in I.1, middle and right corresponding to
the imaginary and real part of the polarizability respectively. Already at a kВӯpoint density around
6Вӯ8, the level of convergence seems acceptable. A well converged system should however have
a kВӯpoint density at least at 12. But the BSE calculation for this system and with the number of
bands stated above puts a limit to the density of 7. Being at an acceptable level of convergence at
6Вӯ8, this is sufficient.
Figure I.1: Left: convergence on the kВӯpoint density in the ground state calculation in the first
calculator with respect to the ground state energy. Middle, right: imaginary and real part of the
polarizability for different kВӯpoint densities.
However, since we have to be somewhat below perfect convergence, we consider what is a suitable
value of the broadening of the peaks, О·. Thus, we compare a well converged result from figure I.1,
more specifically the one with a density of 16, and then we made 4 finite RPA calculations where
the kВӯpoint density was kept at 7, and where we varied the broadening from 50 to 200 meV, to see
which one of them matches the well converged plot the most. The result is shown in figure I.2.
112
==PAGE:119==
Figure I.2: The plot shows a convergencetest on the parameter О·. In the real BSE calculations for
this material we can only have a kВӯpoint density of 7, which is below the limit for a well converged
kВӯpoint density at approximately 12. Therefore we compare some finite RPA calculation for the
polarizability at kВӯpoint density of 7 but with different broadenings to a well converged finite RPA
calculation to see which one matched the most.
It is perhaps not possible to draw a very clear conclusion on a suitable broadening from figure I.2.
It may at first glance appear that a О· of 50 meV is closest to the well converged RPA calculation.
However, note that at around 3.25 eV in the plot of the imaginary part, the 50 meV plot dives too
much, at around 3.5 eV it has an extra peak, and the single peak in the well converged case at
around 4.5 eV is three peaks in the 50 meV case. In the real part, it is very wiggly compared to
the well converged case. The differences seem small, but they will have a large effect on the final
hybrid results which will have the wrong shape compared to experimental results. In the other end
of the scale, at 200 meV, much of the information about where the peaks of the imaginary part
of the polarizability are located has vanished, which is not desirable either. Instead, a reasonable
compromise is found in a broadening of 100 meV. The shape resembles the well converged case
with some very small deviations. The peaks Вӯ in both the real and imaginary part Вӯ are of course
not high enough, but the idea is to compensate for that through the full RPA calculation, and in the
finite case, obtaining the right shape of the polarizabilities are of greater importance.
We finally did a convergence test on the full RPA calculation. The calculations were done with an
О· of 50 meV and a plane wave cutoff at 150 eV. Having found an acceptably converged kВӯpoint
density in the finite calculation at around 8, we chose to initially keep the kВӯpoint density in the full
case at 9 and converging on the number of bands. The broadening was put to 50 meV. The real and
imaginary part of the polarizability for different number of bands are shown in figure I.3, upper left
and right. The plot reveals that to obtain convergence up to the 60 eV that we have included, 100
bands needs to be included in the calculations. We then converged on the kВӯpoint density keeping
the number of bands at 100, (I.3), lower left and right. It may be a bit hard to see when perfect
convergence happens if we do not zoom on the axis and so we have only made the plot up to 10
eV. This is because we want to consider the small oscillations. If we were to consider the entire
plot, it would look like it had converged with respect to the number of kВӯpoints immediately. The
plots reveal that perfect convergence is obtained at about a density of 19.
113
==PAGE:120==
Figure I.3: The two upper plots shows a convergence test on the number of bands included in
the full RPA calculation. Here, the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator was kept at fixed at
9. Convergence occurs at around 100 bands. The number of bands was then put to 100, and we
converge the kВӯpoint density, and the result of this is shown on the two lower plots, where we have
zoomed on the axis to be able to see what happens (and of course also checked that nothing else
happens at higher frequencies, but we have not included that here). Perfect convergence occurs at
around a kВӯpoint density of 19.
OutВӯofВӯplane calculations
We also did an outВӯofВӯplane calculation on TiO2, rutile meaning that the incoming EВӯfield was
polarized parallel to the cВӯaxis. This did of course not change the ground state calculation, nor
did it change the number of bands included in the finite calculations when Пүc was chosen to 2 eV.
However, by mistake, we included one more valence band in the calculation. The mistake however
is so small that we donвҖҷt think it made sense to redo all calculations. However, it does increase the
cutoff to 2.1 eV. Thus, Пүc = 3.4eV + 2.1eV=5.5eV. We need to ensure convergence in the second
calculator on the finite and full RPA calculations. In both cases, the plane wave cutoff was put to
100 eV. The two finite calculations had a broadening of 100 meV while the full RPA calculation
had a broadening of 50 meV. Figure I.4 shows the convergence on the finite RPA calculation on the
imaginary part (left) and the real part (right). Convergence happens at about a kВӯpoint density of
8Вӯ10, which is rather low compared to many other materials considered in this project. The the real
BSE calculations we have a kВӯpoint density of 8, and this is then to be considered a well converged
density.

==PAGE:121==
Figure I.4: Left, right: imaginary and real part of the polarizability for different kВӯpoint densities.
We then converged the full RPA calculation with respect to the number of bands, while the kВӯpoint
density was fixed at 9. The calculations were done with an О· of 50 meV and a plane wave cutoff
at 100 eV. The result is shown in figure I.5 with the imaginary part (upper, left) and the real part
(upper, left). Here we see that the system is converged at about 120 bands. We then converged on
the number of kВӯpoints. Convergence happens at about a kВӯpoint density of 15.

I.0.2
Anatase TiO2
The lattice parameters of TiO2 in the anatase phase are found experimentally by [21] to be a =
b = 3.782 Г… and c = 9.502 Г…, and the space group is number 141. The basis of the atomic
positions was found at [24] to be (1/2, 1/2, 1/2), (0, 1/2, 0.457152) and the angles between the
lattice vectors in the conventional unit cell are 90, 90 and 90 degrees. The setup was created using
the ASE spacegroup subpackage [40].
Initially we converged the number of kВӯpoints in the first calculator with respect to the ground state
energy. The result is shown in figure I.6, left. The plot shows that we, even at rather large kВӯpoint
densities, have some deviations from the ground state energy, that the calculation otherwise seems
to have converged to already at a kВӯpoint density of 3. Note however that the energy differences
on the yВӯscale are very small. So it does seems reasonable to conclude that we have convergence
already at a kВӯpoint density of 3.
We then converged the second calculator with respect to the kВӯpoint density needed to be included
in the finite BSE calculation. The calculations were done with an О· of 50 meV and a plane wave
cutoff at 100 eV. From a convergence point of view, anatase TiO2 is one of the more challenging
materials. Proper convergence requires way more kВӯpoints than we are able to include in the BSE
calculation. To compensate a bit, we have only included bands within Оҙ = 1.6 eV on each side of
the band gap, which corresponds to 12 valence bands and 10 conduction bands. We experimented
with including even fewer bands in the calculations, but this results in the excitonic peaks not being
captured properly, and studying the plots we concluded that a compromise on the kВӯpoint density
was to be preferred over including too few bands. Note however that we would normally aim at
having bands within at least 2 eV on each side of the band gap, so we did reduce this number a
bit. The direct band gap was found by fitting the outputted BSE polarizability to the experimental
data, and was found to be about 4.6 eV. Thus, for this system, Пүc = 4.6eV + 1.6eV = 6.2eV.
Figure I.6 shows a convergence test on the kВӯpoint density in finite RPA calculation with a broadВӯ
ening of the 100 meV matching the broadening that we intend to use in the BSE calculation. The
plots reveals that the finite RPA polarizability is only fully converged at kВӯpoint density of 12Вӯ15.
In our real BSE calculations we did not achieve having a larger density than 7. From considering
(I.6) this is perhaps not an awful level of convergence, but we would normally have aimed at more
kВӯpoints had that been possible.
Figure I.6: Left: convergence on the kВӯpoint density in the ground state calculation in the first
calculator with respect to the ground state energy. Middle, right: imaginary and real part of the
polarizability for different kВӯpoint densities.
We finally converged the full RPA calculation. Here we first converged on the number of bands,
while the kВӯpoint density was fixed at 8. The calculations were done with an О· of 50 meV and a
plane wave cutoff at 100 eV. The result is shown in figure I.7 with the imaginary part (upper, left)
and the real part (upper, left). Here we see that the system is converged at about 170 bands. We
then converged on the number of kВӯpoints. Convergence happens at about a kВӯpoint density of 20.
116
==PAGE:123==
Figure I.7: The two upper plots shows a convergence test on the number of bands included in
the full RPA calculation. Here, the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator was kept at fixed at
8. Convergence occurs at around 170 bands. The number of bands was then put to 170, and we
convergence on the kВӯpoint density, and the result of this is shown on the two lower plots. Once
again, we have zoomed on the axis to be able to see what happens. Perfect convergence occurs at
around a kВӯpoint density of 20.
I.0.3
MoS2
The lattice parameters of bulk 2HВӯ MoS2, which is the most stable phase of bulk MoS2, are found
experimentally in [29] to be a = b = 3.161 Г… and c = 12.295 Г…. The space group number is 194,
and the atomic positions can be described in the basis (2/3, 1/3, 1/4), (2/3, 1/3, 0.633082). The
angles between the lattice vectors are 90, 90 and 120 degrees. The setup was created using the
ASE spacegroup subpackage [40].
Initially, we converged the ground state density. The result is shown in figure I.8, left. This reveals
that we have convergence at a kВӯpoint density of 5.
The plane wave cutoff of the BSE, finite RPA and full RPA calculations is 100 eV, and the О· = 100
meV for the finite BSE calculation. For this material we are able to include bands with Оҙ = 2.5 eV
on each side of the band gap corresponding to 4 valence bands and 8 conduction bands. The direct
band gap was found by fitting the outputted BSE polarizability to the experimental data, and was
found to be about 2 eV. Thus, for this system, Пүc = 2eV + 2.5eV = 4.5eV.
We then made a kВӯpoint density convergence test on the finite RPA calculation with the same О·
(and all other settings). The result is shown in figure I.8 middle (imaginary part) and right (real
part) where we have plotted different values of the kВӯpoint density. The test revels that convergence
happens at about a kВӯpoint density of 12. We were however only able to perform BSE calculations
with a kВӯpoint density of 8. A kВӯpoint density of 6 had, in our own assessment of the plots in figure
I.8, not been sufficient, but just at 8 we believe that the degree of convergence is acceptable.
117
==PAGE:124==
Figure I.8: Left: convergence on the kВӯpoint density in the ground state calculation in the first
calculator with respect to the ground state energy. Middle, right: imaginary and real part of the
polarizability for different kВӯpoint densities.
We then converged the second calculator for the full RPA calculation with respect to the number
of bands, while the kВӯpoint density was fixed at 10. The calculations were done with an О· of 50
meV and a plane wave cutoff at 100 eV. The result is shown in figure I.9, the two upper plots. The
system is fully converged at about a 100 bands. We then converged the full RPA calculation with
respect to the number of kВӯpoints while keeping the number of bands fixed at 110. The result is
shown in figure I.9, the two lower plots. Perfect convergence happens at around a kВӯpoint density
of 20. There is zoom on the axis on this plot like for the previous materials.
Figure I.9: The two upper plots shows a convergence test on the number of bands included in
the full RPA calculation. Here, the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator was kept at fixed at
10. Convergence occurs at around 100 bands. The number of bands was then put to 110, and we
convergence on the kВӯpoint density, and the result of this is shown on the two lower plots. Once
again, we have zoomed on the axis to be able to see what happens. Perfect convergence occurs at
around a kВӯpoint density of 20.
118
==PAGE:125==
I.0.4
hBN
The lattice constants of hBN are found experimentally in [30] to be a = b = 2.50583 Г… and
c = 6.6090 Г… at a temperature of 98 K which is close to the temperature which we are doing
the calculations at: remember that we have decided to work with a FermiВӯDirac occupation of
0.01 corresponding to a temperature of 116 K. The space group number is 194, and the atomic
positions can be described in the basis (1/3, 2/3, 1/4), (1/3, 2/3, 3/4). The angles between the
lattice vectors are 90, 90 and 120 degrees. The setup was created using the ASE spacegroup
subpackage [40].
For this material, we included bands within Оҙ = 2.9 eV to each side of the band gap corresponding
to 5 valence bands and 2 conduction bands. The direct band gap was found by fitting the outputted
BSE polarizability to the experimental data, and was found to be about 6.7 eV. Thus, for this system,
Пүc = 6.7 eV + 2.9 eV = 9.6 eV.
The convergence on the number of kВӯpoints in the first calculator is shown on figure I.10, left.
Convergence occurs at a kВӯpoint density of 4. We then converged on the number of kВӯpoints needed
for the BSE calculation by considering the finite RPA calculation with О· = 100 meV and a plane
wave cutoff at 100 eV. Figure I.10, middle (imaginary part) and right (real part) of the finite RPA
polarizability respectively for different values of the kВӯpoint density. Convergence happens at
about a kВӯpoint density of 14Вӯ16. In the real BSE calculation we had a kВӯpoint density of 8, which
is perhaps a bit low considering the plots, but does seems acceptable.
Figure I.10: Left: convergence on the kВӯpoint density in the ground state calculation in the first
calculator with respect to the ground state energy. Middle, right: imaginary and real part of the
polarizability for different kВӯpoint densities.
We then did a convergence on the full RPA with respect to the number of bands. The calculations
were done with an О· of 50 meV and a plane wave cutoff at 100 eV. The result is shown in figure I.11.
We see that compared to the other materials considered in the project, hBN converges rather fast
with respect to the number of bands included, already around 40 bands. We then kept the number
of bands at 40 and converged on the kВӯpoint density. Here, on the other hand, perfect convergence
happens rather lately, above approximately a kВӯpoint density of 30.
119
==PAGE:126==
Figure I.11: The two upper plots shows a convergence test on the number of bands included in
the full RPA calculation. Here, the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator was kept at fixed at 8.
Convergence occurs at around 40 bands. The number of bands was then put slightly above this,
at 60 bands, and we convergence on the kВӯpoint density, and the result of this is shown on the two
lower plots. Once again, we have zoomed on the axis to be able to see what happens. Perfect
convergence occurs at around a kВӯpoint density of above 30.
I.0.5
Si
We here show some convergence tests on bulk Si. The data shown in figure 6.3 was obtained from
one of my supervisors, Mark Svendsen, and was used in his work, [2], since the point of the figure
was to show that the combination of shifting to eigenvalues to match the band gap and a subsequent
Hilbert transform of the BSE calculation results in a theoretical prediction which almost exactly
matches the experiment removing the need Вӯ in the case of Si Вӯ to do any other corrections, like the
FВӯsum correction which was made in his work. I have not received the details on the settings of his
calculation. But it will not make a large difference, since we can make calculations very similar to
those in figure 6.3.mq ls
However, an important point is that for Si, the real part of the polarizability is converged already
at about 8 bands, which is what we see in figure I.12, upper right. We have also made convergence
testS on the number of kВӯpoints shown in the two lower plots.

==PAGE:127==
Figure I.12: The two upper plots shows a convergence test on the number of bands included in
the full RPA calculation. Here, the kВӯpoint density in the second calculator was kept at fixed at 5.
Convergence occurs at around 8 bands. The number of bands was then put somewhat higher to be
completely sure Вӯ to 16 Вӯ and we convergence on the kВӯpoint density, and the result of this is shown
on the two lower plots. Once again, we have zoomed on the axis to be able to see what happens.
Perfect convergence occurs at around a kВӯpoint density of 20.
I.0.6
HfS2
The work on HfS2 has been made in close collaboration with one of my supervisors, Mark SvendВӯ
sen. The research section, at which this project has been made, has a collaboration with a neighВӯ
boring experimental department, which has bought a HfS2 crystal that they intend to measure at
least the refractive index on. Thus, we would like to make predictions on the refractive index for
this material. We had hoped to get the experimental data before this report was handed in, but there
was not enough time for the experimental department to finish its work.
The lattice parameters of HfS2 were found by with a DFT calculation with a soВӯcalled D3 relaxation
by my supervisor. He found them to be a = b = 3.626965 Г… and c = 5.882979 Г…. The space group
number is 164, and the atomic positions can be described in the basis (0, 0, 0), (2/3, 1/3, 0.763952).
The angles between the lattice vectors are 90, 90 and 120 degrees. He also sent me the finite BSE
and the full RPA calculations, so there has not been any need to converge on the full RPA calculaВӯ
tion.
To save time, we did not make a convergence test on the finite RPA calculation. The number of
bands should of course match the BSE calculation, and having done numerous kВӯpoint density
convergence tests at this point for other materials, we just made a finite RPA calculation with a
high kВӯpoint density of 20.
The settings of the BSE calculations was as follows: The first calculator had a kВӯpoint density of
1. The occupation was put to 0.05, i.e. a bit larger than has been the setting for the other materials
121
==PAGE:128==
considered in this project. This corresponds to a temperature of 580 K. The second calculator had
a kВӯpoint density of 6, and a occupation of 0.0001 (1.16 K), i.e. a bit lower than we had normally
done in this project. I did make a new calculation Вӯ i.e. I did not use MarkвҖҷs BSE calculation Вӯ
because I wanted the calculation to go up to higher frequencies due to the way some of the hybrids
are constructed. But otherwise, the calculations settings were the same.
For this material, we included bands within Оҙ = 2.6 eV to each side of the band gap corresponding
to 4 valence bands and 3 conduction bands. As mentioned previously, we do in general not know
the correct value for the band gap of the material, and have decided to just fit the BSE excitonic
peaks to the excitonic peaks in the experimental datasets. Since we do not have an experimental
dataset, my supervisor Mark made a GW calculation to make a better prediction for the band gap.
He found it to be 2.9 eV. Thus, for this system, Пүc = 2.9eV + 2.6eV = 5.5eV.
